###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Manchester "joke" was Re: History of COBOL

_(thread depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-15T15:48:35+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8hpto4FgkbU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4c0f9489-ab9f-4e52-a5fa-49a76422a93c@n40g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <i90d3c$5j6$1@reader1.panix.com> <8hhu98Fj4kU1@mid.individual.net> <b49afb0a-f461-4a54-9208-c35c1414fa64@l20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <8hj5r0FlmsU1@mid.individual.net> <5f78661e-a410-43b3-8190-4b8b3f6e064c@i5g2000yqe.googlegroups.com> <8hm442FpecU1@mid.individual.net> <8uCdnYSa8N9deCjRnZ2dnUVZ_oWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <8hn48iFlbaU1@mid.individual.net> <h8ieb6hvhgbpnki6t0isrioq0cfvitspcf@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
<snipped>>>
>> A book, of which at least two movies were made with the key words
>> "Manchester" and "Candidate".
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

Oh Dear!

I have had non-American friends say to me on a number of occasions: 
"Subtlety is lost on the Yanks."

On all of these occasions I have defended the people of a place I enjoy 
visiting and where I count a number of friends.

Doc, (who is undeniably American, by residence if not by birth) made a 
gentle joke involving a Malapropism on "Manchester". It was not obvious but 
it was amusing (I guess being amused is subjective - Damn! I'm trying not to 
write this with emoticons but I was grinning when I wrote that...)

I followed this with some weak puns on the author's name, mainly so Doc 
could see his effort was not wasted and at least one person had appreciated 
it.

Why is it all so difficult?

Are things not funny "in passing"?

Is someone falling over or getting a pie in the face the only reason for 
laughter? (Don't get me wrong; I enjoy slapstick humour just like everyone 
else, but sometimes a more subtle approach has more flavour to it.)

It obviously isn't about nationalities.

One of the things which REALLY made me laugh recently (a very simple "down 
home" example...)

I am watching the movie "King Kong" (Peter Jackson's re-make) with some 
friends  who have a small, absolutely endearing, Bichon-Maltese cross 
("Thomas"). Thomas is a great fan of animals on TV. (He especially loves the 
Black Horse in some Bank ads. I believe he entertains delusions of humping 
the horse, but who knows what doggie dreams run through his little head and 
it would be far from me to rain on his parade...) Anyway, instead of running 
to the screen and barking an invitation to hump (as he does with most 
animals...) when Kong appears in all his might, little Thomas scurries for 
cover behind the couch. (He eventually came out and settled himself securely 
in his owner's lap for the remainder of the movie.)

This afforded us no little amusement. It is kind of slapstick, but the best 
is yet to come...

Comes the part where the mortally wounded ape is tragically dying and 
finally crashes to the ground. Thomas jumps off his secure lap and rushes 
barking to the screen.

And the bit that REALLY cracked me up?

The dry, almost unobserved observation from another person in the room: "Oh 
sure, you're a BIG dog now..."

I relay this as an attempt to show that (for me at least...) humour works at 
many levels and it doesn't have to be obvious.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Manchester "joke" was Re: History of COBOL

_(thread depth: 16)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2010-10-15T06:03:55+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<i98qsb$auo$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<4c0f9489-ab9f-4e52-a5fa-49a76422a93c@n40g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <8hn48iFlbaU1@mid.individual.net> <h8ieb6hvhgbpnki6t0isrioq0cfvitspcf@4ax.com> <8hpto4FgkbU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <8hpto4FgkbU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>Clark F Morris wrote:
><snipped>>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>"Subtlety is lost on the Yanks."

I had an American friend who commented that during his World War II days 
he could always tell who was sitting where in a moving-picture audience as 
the Americans would laugh at the 'normal parts' and the Brits would 
laugh... well, he actually used the phrase 'at the funniest things' but 
caught himself and changed it to 'at the strangest stuff'.

>
>On all of these occasions I have defended the people of a place I enjoy 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>Are things not funny "in passing"?

Mr Dashwood, those who have studied Plato to a depth greater than 
superficial have run across what was called 'The Theory of Forms', in 
which an object manifested a quality insofar as that object manifested a 
connection with the form (Gr. 'eidos') of that quality.  A 'good table' 
and a 'good joke' are good only in that they both manifest a similar kind 
of connection and partake of the Form of the Good, an action showed 
justice because it partook of the Form of the Just, et and cetera, und so 
weiter.

Socrates did *not*, to the best of my recollection, make any mention of 
the Form of the Funny.  There are many possible reasons for this but 
perhaps the most likely is the most obvious: it doesn't exist.

(pause for laughter)

>
>Is someone falling over or getting a pie in the face the only reason for 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>It obviously isn't about nationalities.

It may be... and it may not be.  Slapstick humor (to get back to Plato I 
believe there was a point in The Republic where Socrates mentions how 
everyone laughs when a person is about to sit down and someone snatches 
the chair away... Big Laffs, even in the Oldene Dayse!) is non-verbal 
communication and once one starts to deal with language an entirely 
different set of customs, both learned and physical (parts of the brain 
involved), are involved.

I'd go on here but... humor is something I enjoy and the more one analyses 
it the less it seems to appear.  To avoid that perhaps another observation 
deserves attention:

Yes, I noticied the halfword-congruence between 'Manch-ester' and 
'Manch-urian' and associated it with a decades-old talkie (and the recent 
remake thereof); some people (at least one) were able to see this and 
found it caused them to smile and others did not.  Nothing new about that, 
either, some folks find some things funny while others don't.

Now... which would it be most pleasant to be, one who finds a bit of humor 
in an unexpected place and enjoys it, one who does not find humor in such 
a manner and passes by, with a figurative blindness... or one who *senses* 
that Others are Having A Good Time and demands that this sort of behavior 
be accounted for, right away?

In the last case it might be that the unknowing-one could, in time, learn 
to see how a particular phenomenon is actually partaking of the Form of 
the Funny and have a Life enlarged by being able to see More Funny 
Stuff... or it could be that another joke would have to be explained and 
lost.

(can't seem to get away from that dratted Socrates... the dialogue Meno 
begins with (Jowett translation, 
http://www.gutenberg.org/cache/epub/1643/pg1643.txt ) Meno asking the 
multiple question of :

'Can you tell me, Socrates, whether virtue is acquired by teaching or by 
practice; or if neither by teaching nor by practice, then whether it comes 
to man by nature, or in what other way?'

... and now, imagine just a *slight* change... and it becomes 'Can you 
tell me, Socrates, whether a sense of humor is acquired by teaching or by 
practice;, or if neither... then whether it comes to man by nature, or in 
what other way?'

DD
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8hr3gjF7vtU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<4c0f9489-ab9f-4e52-a5fa-49a76422a93c@n40g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <8hn48iFlbaU1@mid.individual.net> <h8ieb6hvhgbpnki6t0isrioq0cfvitspcf@4ax.com> <8hpto4FgkbU1@mid.individual.net> <i98qsb$auo$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <8hpto4FgkbU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[109 more quoted lines elided]…
> DD

An interesting discourse. Of course the Ancient Greeks, not having 
television, found ways to amuse themselves...(I still marvel at them 
calculating the distance to the sun by sticking a couple of sticks in the 
beach and measuring the shadows... It is quite incredible what people 
actually knew before they forgot, and had to have it all re-invented 
again...We think we are so smart with our technology, but the power of real 
thought may be a dying thing.)

Let's not forget that poor Old Socrates came to a sticky end, even though he 
apparently handled it with quiet dignity. I did remember reading some of 
what you described, a very long time ago, but, being young and fit and it 
being a sunny day, I'm afraid I rather glazed over and started contemplating 
the ladies in the class without their clothes on... A triumph of Form over 
Fabric.

Flippancy aside, you raise an interesting question: Where DOES our sense of 
humour come from?

We all think we have one.

You often see "GSOH" in personal ads or people looking for dates. I wonder 
if there is such a thing as a BAD SOH?

My Father was a very funny man with a gentle sense of humour and an 
infectious laugh; my Mother loved a joke but had difficulty with subtlety. 
My father and I would be in stitches at "The Goon Show" while my Mother had 
no idea what was going on, or why we found it hilarious. My Father tried to 
explain it to her but she never "got it"... Still, she was happy that we 
enjoyed it.

I don't think it is genetic.

I've observed a similar thing with Monty Python; girls don't generally enjoy 
it. I had a girl friend who was besotted with "Absolutely Fabulous". 
Fortunately, I enjoyed it too and we could watch it together, but when it 
came to Harry Enfield I would be on my own. (I think that Enfield, 
Whitehouse, and Jennifer Saunders) possibly also Dawn French, are just comic 
geniuses for this generation. I still miss the old Britsh shows (Two 
Ronnies, Morecambe and Wise, even Benny Hill...).

Looking across all the "funny" things I enjoy and searching for common 
threads doesn't turn up much.

I see a marked lack of wit in stand up comedy these days. They seem to try 
and substitute shouting and swearing in place of it, but it isn't the same. 
I remember seeing a very young stand up guy at the Comedy Store in Los 
Angeles many years ago. I went with friends, we had a few beers and this guy 
just left us helpless with laughter. We were all convinced he would be 
famous (at the time he was unknown).  It was Robin Williams.

I'm inclined to agree with you Doc, that analysing humour may not be a good 
thing to do.

For many years I had printed on my personal cards: "Those without a sense of 
humour are at the mercy of the rest of us."

(Nowadays all my cards are business cards and I have been working very hard 
to establish credibility for PRIMA, so it probably isn't appropriate :-))

Pete.
```

Regarding what is funny to Americans and what is funny to the "other"
English speaking folks on this tiny planet -

I was on assignment in Wellington New Zealand when the Tom Hanks
movie, "Forrest Gump" came out.  I took a Sunday and went to a theatre
in Wellington to see it.

I must've been the only American in the house as I was laughing out
loud at all of the places where Forrest was superimposed over meetings
with Presidents Kennedy, Johnson and Nixon.  Apparently the local
Kiwi's didn't see the humor in those historical encounters.  They did,
however, laugh at other parts of the movie which were indeed humorous.

- **References:** `<4c0f9489-ab9f-4e52-a5fa-49a76422a93c@n40g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <8hn48iFlbaU1@mid.individual.net> <h8ieb6hvhgbpnki6t0isrioq0cfvitspcf@4ax.com> <8hpto4FgkbU1@mid.individual.net> <i98qsb$auo$1@reader1.panix.com> <hhqgb6t4c80ac3s19c5sk6itid18k0t00l@4ax.com> <bgvgb61in5js9qf3udeua09vr4bfk7vh2o@4ax.com>`

```
On Oct 16, 5:19 am, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
> On Fri, 15 Oct 2010 08:51:21 -0600, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net>
> wrote:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> however, laugh at other parts of the movie which were indeed humorous.

My analysis of the experiences that cause laughter seem to show that
this is not about 'humour' but about conflict: internal conflict; and
the resolution of that. A pun, for example, causes the brain to come
up with two or more meanings and this causes tension. The resolution
of that conflict causes relief and laughter.

As an Americam you would have seen all those films of meetings. The
addition of Forest Gump into them would cause a conflict between your
memory of those clips and what you were seeing. The realisation of it
being faked for the purposes of making the film resolved the conflict
and caused you to laugh.

New Zealanders would not have so much memories of those meetings and
so no, or less, conflict. Anyway NZ audiences are much less
responsive.

The main difference that I see, in general, between english/nz comedy
and american is that the former laughs about themselves while the
latter tends to laugh at the expense of others.
```

There was a lady from Carolina,

Alistair Maclean wrote:
>
> What on earth are you all going on about? I am not aware of any film
> called or featuring Manchester.

Exactly. On the train from Manchester to London, two natives asked us what 
we were doing in Manchester.

"Tourists," I replied.

They looked at each other for quite some time then looked at me as if I had 
just eaten a bug.

"Tourist? In Manchester?" one asked.  "What on earth is there to see in 
Manchester?"

"Oh, you'd be surprised," I said.

Never did tell them I was just dropping off a rental car...
```

```
On Oct 13, 7:41 am, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
> Alistair Maclean wrote:
>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> Never did tell them I was just dropping off a rental car...

The Manchester Bus Museum (Museum of Transport) has a large and varied
collection.

The wife insisted on going to the Coronation Street set, actually they
make lots of different stuff there. I took the kids to a Sooty show.
Closed to the public now.

I spent a whole day at The Museum Of Science And Industry. A great
aircraft hall full of interesting stuff (especially Avro of course),
the first purpose build railway station, Stephenson's Rocket
reproduction and many other railway stuff.

(I should mention that the Buses and S&I were made without the wife on
board).

Just to keep it almost on topic the S&I have a replica of the
Manchester Small-Scale Experimental Machine (SSEM), nicknamed Baby,
which was the world's first stored-program computer.
```

```
On Oct 14, 2:35 am, Kerry Liles
<"kerry.liles[minus_the_spam]"@gmail.com> wrote:
> On 10/12/2010 5:45 PM, Richard wrote:
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Great, but did the SSEM run COBOL?

No, but Ferranti had a big association with Manchester and there is a
Ferranti Pegasus at S&I. Ferranti's association with COBOL is:

"""The Orion is also notable for the use of its own high-level
business language, NEBULA. Nebula was created because of Ferranti's
perception that the COBOL  standard of 1960 was not sufficiently
powerful enough for their machines, notably as COBOL was developed in
the context of decimal, character-oriented batch processing, while
Orion was a binary word-oriented multiprogramming system. NEBULA
adapted many of COBOL's basic concepts, adding new ones of their own.
NEBULA was later ported to the Atlas as well."""
```

There is a popular misconception that Babbage's machine was the first 
computer; it wasn't, but again, it depends on how you define "computer". A 
bronze machine that calculated the precise position of the planets at any 
given point in time, for casting horoscopes, was recovered from the seabed 
near Greece, during the early part of the last century. Painstakingly 
analyzed and reconstructed, it proved to be an astonishing piece of 
equipment that was at least 2000 years 
old...(http://www.dreamscape.com/morgana/triton2.htm)

I have been finding Richard's quotes on historical aspects of computing to 
be authoritative and interesting. Hence my suggestion, although you seem to 
have missed "in the 20th Century"... :-)

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Oct 15, 5:27 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[66 more quoted lines elided]…
> would be a poor account.

Not at all. It would simply be on topic.

If you were to write a book about say, "Cinema in the 1970s", would it be a 
poor book if it didn't mention Harold Lloyd or Jean Harlow?

Should it investigate the use of silver nitrate, celluloid, and mention 
Daguerre?

You COULD outline the process whereby images are captured and appear to 
move, but it wouldn't necessarily be a "poor account" if you didn't. Not 
every book has to be a text book. (Some of us read books for fun, as well as 
information... :-))

Similarly with computers. The kind of person who would be interested in a 
history of computing throughout the 20th century would be someone who has 
probably had some association with it and would like a reference book of 
details, developments, and interesting stories. Explaining the use of binary 
notation in chapter one, can be left to other text books.

The fact is that in this day and age people know what a computer is.

I could see Richard writing something that would be a useful reference 
(maybe with a timeline in the appendix, showing when various machines and 
OSes came into being) and, at the same time, an interesting recreational 
reading book, not a text on the history of computing since the world began.

Pete.
```

## Thread: A COBOL conundrum — 1 msgs

### Re: A COBOL conundrum

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-04T10:55:37-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d2d24c75-04df-45eb-b867-82d772484274@h40g2000pro.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<rMGdnWBe6MzAcDvRnZ2dnUVZ5oydnZ2d@giganews.com> <wWDpo.213048$Bh.187055@en-nntp-12.dc1.easynews.com> <34idnSF6de3jFzrRnZ2dnUVZ5gSdnZ2d@giganews.com> <i88sji$hqu$1@news.eternal-september.org> <8gqr9gF5uqU1@mid.individual.net> <i8a24k$o1o$1@news.eternal-september.org> <8grt7dFi59U1@mid.individual.net> <da6792ce-f9d7-4b1c-a151-13c409aae412@a4g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <8gtnrrFhojU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Oct 4, 11:16 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Richard wrote:
> > On Oct 4, 6:35 am, "Pete Dashwood"
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> that HAD no stack.  (IBM 1401 and later System 360/370).

I am not sure what your point is here. Are you just agreeing that the
IBM 1400s and mainframes are inefficient and archaic architectures ?

> Certainly it is true that people like myself who were required to transit
> from Assembler to COBOL accepted altering instructions in core as a matter
> of course.

The ICL 1900 series did not have a stack either. It used self-
modifying code for a PERFORM putting the return branch in the 'EXIT'
statement (or the implied EXIT statement) and branching to the start
point.

In other words the PERFORM with XE13, XE20, and XEKB worked like an
ALTER and a GO TO but actually did it with less instructions and
cleaned up afterwards, putting a null op in the 'EXIT' so that it
would drop through when not under the control of a PERFORM as per the
language spec. This is why it is a 'high-level' language, it explains
what is to happen and hides _how_ it happens.

Of course on stack machines the PERFORM can work differently. ALTER
can too, especially where self-modifying code can't work. This then
could be less efficient than the PERFORM.
```

---

<a id="t-dbc87d2f5f"></a>
## Thread: A COBOL conundrum — 1 msgs

### Re: A COBOL conundrum

- **From:** Fred Mobach <fred@mobach.nl>
- **Date:** 2010-10-05T13:35:37+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4cab0d8a$0$23765$14726298@news.sunsite.dk>`
- **References:** `<rMGdnWBe6MzAcDvRnZ2dnUVZ5oydnZ2d@giganews.com> <wWDpo.213048$Bh.187055@en-nntp-12.dc1.easynews.com> <34idnSF6de3jFzrRnZ2dnUVZ5gSdnZ2d@giganews.com> <i88sji$hqu$1@news.eternal-september.org> <8gqr9gF5uqU1@mid.individual.net> <i8a24k$o1o$1@news.eternal-september.org> <8grt7dFi59U1@mid.individual.net> <da6792ce-f9d7-4b1c-a151-13c409aae412@a4g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <8gtnrrFhojU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:

> Richard wrote:
>> On Oct 4, 6:35 am, "Pete Dashwood"
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> as a matter of course.

While the IBM 360/370 range and likes already offered the EX(ecute)
operation which took care for memory modification the need for self
modifying instructions in Assembler for me diminished soon. From my
Siemens BS2000 Assembler Instructions (BS2000/OSD) manual :

Execute
Function
The EX instruction executes another instruction. This instruction may be
modified beforehand.
The condition code is changed only if it is changed by the executed
instruction.

But, the use of ALTER in that age could only be done by changing to
another use of the COBOL language.
```

<a id="t-8db0a9c3b2"></a>
## Thread: NetCobol - How make auto-clear in Accept numeric field? — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### NetCobol - How make auto-clear in Accept numeric field?

- **From:** Gilberto Strapazon <gilbertopb@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-10-08T07:07:10-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d726798e-516f-4be6-9e31-43140174bf4e@w19g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi

I searched a lot but didn´t find how to solve this.

We are converting from Microfocus Cobol to Fujitsu NetCobol.
The programs will keep the original console character interface. This
means, display/accept for all, using scrren section also.

The problem is Netcobol Accept appears to not have the auto-clear
feature for the field input and the user have to manually clean all
the field. For character field (PIC X) there´s no problem, but for
numeric fields this is a worst and a risk or error.

Just to detail:

In all the fiels I make an ACCEPT, I want to show the contents of that
field and,
  1) if the user just press ENTER, keep that contents,
  2) if the user writes something, i.e, when press first value key,
the field content is cleaned to receive the new input.

In the Microfocus Cobol there´s the ADISCTRL, but we have not find
nothing similar in Fujitsu NetCobol. Appears almost examples we have
found, is people using NetBios for massive batch or as CGI,

Thanks in advance for your sugestions


Gilberto Strapazon
Porto Alegre

http://cwconnect.computerworld.com.br/zenta
http://gilbertostrapazon.blogspot.com
```

#### ↳ Re: NetCobol - How make auto-clear in Accept numeric field?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-09T12:05:44+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8h9meaFbreU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<d726798e-516f-4be6-9e31-43140174bf4e@w19g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Gilberto Strapazon wrote:
> Hi
>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> http://gilbertostrapazon.blogspot.com

Processing using screen sections is an archaic way to do things, but 
sometimes people have no choice. As you are converting from Micro Focus you 
are probably tied to the screen sections in the legacy programs.

For interactive data entry you would be much better off using PowerCOBOL 
(and it gives much more control over the data fields because they are true 
Windows controls, so you have access to all their properties, colours, text 
fonts, etc.).

Given that you really have no option, try adding "FULL" to your numeric 
field definitions in the screen section. This may give somethng closer to 
the behaviour you are seeking.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-eb9c96e98f"></a>
## Thread: Newbie-ish question about PICTURE IS — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Tutorials, books, learning`](topics.md#learning)_

### Newbie-ish question about PICTURE IS

- **From:** axtens <bruce.axtens@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-10-11T20:15:26-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4d9c17be-24b8-44ed-815f-0fd4ffc1598c@u5g2000prn.googlegroups.com>`

```
The last time I did anything of note with COBOL was in the mid 1980s.
It's been a long long time and I've forgotten more than I ever knew.

That said, can someone explain to me why the REDEFINES in the code
below doesn't hide the leading zeroes, but the MOVE to display-counter
does.

Kind regards,
Bruce.

identification division.
program-id. ztest.
author. Bruce Axtens.
data division.
working-storage section.
01	counters.
	05	counter1	picture is 99.
	88	is-zero value 0.
	05	counter-display redefines counter1 picture is ZZ.
01	display-counter	picture is ZZ.

procedure division.
100-main section.
	perform with test after varying counter1 from 10 by -1 until is-zero
		display counter1 " " with no advancing
		display counter-display " " with no advancing
		move counter1 to display-counter
		display display-counter
	end-perform.
	stop run.
	end-program.


C:\OpenCobol>ztest
10 10 10
09 09  9
08 08  8
07 07  7
06 06  6
05 05  5
04 04  4
03 03  3
02 02  2
01 01  1
00 00
```

#### ↳ Re: Newbie-ish question about PICTURE IS

- **From:** "robertwessel2@yahoo.com" <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2010-10-11T20:53:07-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4baa074d-e699-4c3f-8670-b39903c1cf96@z28g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<4d9c17be-24b8-44ed-815f-0fd4ffc1598c@u5g2000prn.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Oct 11, 10:15 pm, axtens <bruce.axt...@gmail.com> wrote:
> The last time I did anything of note with COBOL was in the mid 1980s.
> It's been a long long time and I've forgotten more than I ever knew.
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> 00 00



Somewhat simplified, the formatting happens when you move data to the
field.  So when you move a new value to counter1, it's formatted as
pic 99 at that time.  The pic ZZ field is only read, and that happens
largely like a character field, so the ZZ in counter-display never has
an opportunity to do anything.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Newbie-ish question about PICTURE IS

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2010-10-12T05:51:51-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<j5Xso.266870$mN7.3277@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<4d9c17be-24b8-44ed-815f-0fd4ffc1598c@u5g2000prn.googlegroups.com> <4baa074d-e699-4c3f-8670-b39903c1cf96@z28g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`

```
<robertwessel2@yahoo.com> wrote in message 
news:4baa074d-e699-4c3f-8670-b39903c1cf96@z28g2000yqh.googlegroups.com...
On Oct 11, 10:15 pm, axtens <bruce.axt...@gmail.com> wrote:
> The last time I did anything of note with COBOL was in the mid 1980s.
> It's been a long long time and I've forgotten more than I ever knew.
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> 00 00



.> Somewhat simplified, the formatting happens when you move data to the
> field.  So when you move a new value to counter1, it's formatted as
> pic 99 at that time.  The pic ZZ field is only read, and that happens
> largely like a character field, so the ZZ in counter-display never has
> an opportunity to do anything.

Another way to think about this is to remember that a REDEFINES *never* 
changes the data in a field.  It is just another way of looking at the data 
that is there.  (As well as determining how that data is received and sent)
```

## Thread: Before I submit this to RosettaCode ... — 7 msgs

### Before I submit this to RosettaCode ...

- **From:** "Bruce M. Axtens" <bruce.axtens@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-10-12T16:39:46+08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<H8-dnbWyN55GgynRnZ2dnUVZ7sidnZ2d@westnet.com.au>`

```
Before I submit this to RosettaCode is this a good way of doing it, or 
am I barking up the wrong brick wall? This is the classic 99 Bottles of 
Beer program. I haven't been to the 99bob site to see what was done there.

Kind regards,
Bruce.

IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
PROGRAM-ID. NINETY-NINE.
AUTHOR. BRUCE AXTENS.
ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
DATA DIVISION.
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
01	COUNTER	PICTURE IS 99.
	88 TWO-BOTTLES-LEFT VALUE 2.
	88 ONE-BOTTLE-LEFT VALUE 1.
	88 NO-BOTTLES-LEFT VALUE 0.

01	PARTS-OF-COUNTER REDEFINES COUNTER.
	05	TENS		PICTURE IS 9.
	05	DIGITS	PICTURE IS 9.

01	AFTER-TEN-WORDS.
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE SPACES.
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE "TWENTY".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE "THIRTY".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE "FORTY".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE "FIFTY".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE "SIXTY".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE "SEVENTY".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE "EIGHTY".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE "NINETY".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(7) VALUE SPACES.

01	AFTER-TEN-ARRAY REDEFINES AFTER-TEN-WORDS.
	05	ATENS OCCURS 10 TIMES PIC X(7).

01	DIGIT-WORDS.
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "ONE".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "TWO".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "THREE".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "FOUR".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "FIVE".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "SIX".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "SEVEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "EIGHT".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "NINE".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "TEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "ELEVEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "TWELVE".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "THIRTEEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "FOURTEEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "FIFTEEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "SIXTEEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "SEVENTEEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "EIGHTEEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE "NINETEEN".
	05	FILLER	PIC X(9) VALUE SPACES.
	
01	DIGIT-ARRAY REDEFINES DIGIT-WORDS.
	05	ADIGITS OCCURS 20 TIMES 	PIC X(9).
	
01	NUMBER-NAME PIC X(15).

PROCEDURE DIVISION.
100-MAIN SECTION.
100-SETUP.
	PERFORM VARYING COUNTER FROM 99 BY -1 UNTIL NO-BOTTLES-LEFT
		PERFORM 100-SHOW-NUMBER
		DISPLAY " OF BEER ON THE WALL"
		PERFORM 100-SHOW-NUMBER
		DISPLAY " OF BEER"
		DISPLAY "TAKE " WITH NO ADVANCING
		IF COUNTER = 1
			DISPLAY "IT " WITH NO ADVANCING
		ELSE
			DISPLAY "ONE " WITH NO ADVANCING
		END-IF
		DISPLAY "DOWN AND PASS IT ROUND"
		SUBTRACT 1 FROM COUNTER GIVING COUNTER
		PERFORM 100-SHOW-NUMBER
		DISPLAY " OF BEER ON THE WALL"
		ADD 1 TO COUNTER GIVING COUNTER
		DISPLAY SPACE
	END-PERFORM.

	STOP RUN.
	
100-SHOW-NUMBER.
	IF COUNTER = 0
		DISPLAY "NO" WITH NO ADVANCING
	ELSE
		IF COUNTER < 20
			DISPLAY FUNCTION TRIM( ADIGITS( COUNTER ) ) WITH NO ADVANCING
		ELSE
			IF COUNTER < 100
				MOVE SPACES TO NUMBER-NAME
				STRING ATENS( TENS ) DELIMITED BY SPACE, SPACE DELIMITED BY SIZE, 
ADIGITS( DIGITS ) DELIMITED BY SPACE INTO NUMBER-NAME
				DISPLAY FUNCTION TRIM( NUMBER-NAME) WITH NO ADVANCING
			END-IF
		END-IF
	END-IF.
	IF COUNTER = 1
		DISPLAY " BOTTLE" WITH NO ADVANCING
	ELSE
		DISPLAY " BOTTLES" WITH NO ADVANCING
	END-IF.

100-END.
END-PROGRAM.
```

When compiled with Microfocus, MD Cobol objects to the TRIM Functions.

On Oct 12, 9:39 am, "Bruce M. Axtens" <bruce.axt...@gmail.com> wrote:
> Before I submit this to RosettaCode is this a good way of doing it, or
> am I barking up the wrong brick wall? This is the classic 99 Bottles of
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.

MF Cobol syntax for TRIM is:

trim
The trim function trims a string value of spaces before and after.

Syntax:
trim(string, [0|1|2])

Parameters:
0 trim spaces both before and after
1 trim spaces before only
2 trim spaces after only
```

Hello Alistair!

13 Oct 10 11:25, Alistair Maclean wrote to All:

 >> Sigh. "TWENTY ONE" should by hyphenated (i.e., "twenty-one").- Hide
 >> quoted text -
 >>

 > It is now fashionable practice in the UK to drop hyphens. Personally,
 > I rarely use hyphens unless it is absolutely necessary to tie two
 > words to ovoid ambiguity.

Unless your processing cheques or other similar instruments.

Vince

## Thread: address exception in Windows in OpenCobol attempting to commit to DB2 database — 2 msgs

_Topics: [`Open-source COBOL (GnuCOBOL, OpenCOBOL, TinyCOBOL)`](topics.md#open-source) · [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases)_

### address exception in Windows in OpenCobol attempting to commit to DB2 database

- **From:** William Lightner <user@compgroups.net/>
- **Date:** 2010-10-14T00:00:18-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<a8-dnbjybrP_EyvRnZ2dnUVZ_sSdnZ2d@giganews.com>`

```
OpenCobol 1.0

At least a couple queries (including an update) succeed before the error.

Precompile script:
SET P1=ACTION REPLACE 
SET P2=BINDFILE
SET P3=PACKAGE USING JBHBAT
SET P4=TARGET ANSI_COBOL
SET P5=OWNER ALIUPDT
SET P6=EXPLAIN YES
SET P7=STRDEL APOSTROPHE
SET P8=DISCONNECT EXPLICIT
SET P9=ISOLATION CS
SET PA=RELEASE COMMIT
SET PB=SYNCPOINT NONE
SET PC=VALIDATE BIND

SET OPTIONS=%P1% %P2% %P3% %P4% %P5% %P6% %P7% %P8% %P9% %PA% %PB% %PC%

DB2 PRECOMPILE %1 %OPTIONS%

OpenCobol compiler options:
-cx -fimplicit-init -fdebugging-line -fstack-check -fstatic-linkage -fdebug -fstatic-call
```

#### ↳ Re: address exception in Windows in OpenCobol attempting to commit to DB2 database

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-15T06:05:26+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8horioFhl5U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<a8-dnbjybrP_EyvRnZ2dnUVZ_sSdnZ2d@giganews.com>`

```
William Lightner wrote:
> OpenCobol 1.0
>
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> -fdebug -fstatic-call

Specifying the Isolation level to be CS (Cursor stability) means that all 
reads are with lock (Committed Read).  This may be conflicting with a 
previous lock (you said you had done an update).

Was the update to a row that will be returned by the cursor? Did you specify 
WITH LOCK before getting it? Check for a row that is known to the cursor or 
will be part of the set fetched by the cursor, and is also being accessed 
independently of the cursor.

The problem is more likely to be with DB2 than with Open COBOL in this 
instance.

Is there transactional processing going on with the cursor?

Did the problem occur on a COMMIT (explicit) or was it on a different 
operation (implicit COMMIT)?

Need much more information to be of help here.

If it were me I'd try a synch point of say, 10 records and see if the 
problem recurs...

Pete.
```

---

<a id="t-05abd14879"></a>
## Thread: Before I submit this to RosettaCode ... — 72 msgs

### Re: Before I submit this to RosettaCode ...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-15T05:41:43+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8hoq6jF8g5U2@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<H8-dnbWyN55GgynRnZ2dnUVZ7sidnZ2d@westnet.com.au> <128392a6-d399-44f2-a360-163091d5568b@z28g2000yqh.googlegroups.com> <scudnRIRq8zTqCnRnZ2dnUVZ8rqdnZ2d@westnet.com.au> <b700e377-173e-4597-9ccc-de20a69b4b1a@30g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <gVZso.175$iA3.20@viwinnwfe02.internal.bigpond.com> <6aqdnSbStud8NynRnZ2dnUVZ_gydnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Bruce M. Axtens wrote:
>> Alistair Maclean wrote:
…[42 more quoted lines elided]…
> Sigh. "TWENTY ONE" should by hyphenated (i.e., "twenty-one").

According to the best style authorities available on written English, ALL of 
the numbers from twenty-one to ninety-nine, apart from those which are 
multiples of ten, SHOULD be hyphenated.

That, of course, is not a big deal programmatically. I'd be more concerned 
about the actual code if I was going to make it public... I haven't analyzed 
it in detail, but I'm pretty sure it could be tighter.

Let's have a look...

IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
PROGRAM-ID. NINETY-NINE.
AUTHOR. BRUCE AXTENS.
ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
DATA DIVISION.
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
01 COUNTER PICTURE IS 99.
88 TWO-BOTTLES-LEFT VALUE 2.
88 ONE-BOTTLE-LEFT VALUE 1.
88 NO-BOTTLES-LEFT VALUE 0.

01 PARTS-OF-COUNTER REDEFINES COUNTER.
05 TENS PICTURE IS 9.
05 DIGITS PICTURE IS 9.

01 AFTER-TEN-WORDS.
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE SPACES.
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "TWENTY".
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "THIRTY".
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "FORTY".
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "FIFTY".
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "SIXTY".
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "SEVENTY".
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "EIGHTY".
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "NINETY".
05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE SPACES.

01 AFTER-TEN-ARRAY REDEFINES AFTER-TEN-WORDS.
05 ATENS OCCURS 10 TIMES PIC X(7).

01 DIGIT-WORDS.
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "ONE".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TWO".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "THREE".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FOUR".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FIVE".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SIX".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SEVEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "EIGHT".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "NINE".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "ELEVEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TWELVE".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "THIRTEEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FOURTEEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FIFTEEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SIXTEEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SEVENTEEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "EIGHTEEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "NINETEEN".
05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE SPACES.

01 DIGIT-ARRAY REDEFINES DIGIT-WORDS.
05 ADIGITS OCCURS 20 TIMES PIC X(9).

01 NUMBER-NAME PIC X(15).

PROCEDURE DIVISION.
100-MAIN SECTION.
100-SETUP.
PERFORM VARYING COUNTER FROM 99 BY -1 UNTIL NO-BOTTLES-LEFT
PERFORM 100-SHOW-NUMBER
DISPLAY " OF BEER ON THE WALL"
PERFORM 100-SHOW-NUMBER
DISPLAY " OF BEER"
DISPLAY "TAKE " WITH NO ADVANCING
IF COUNTER = 1
DISPLAY "IT " WITH NO ADVANCING
ELSE
DISPLAY "ONE " WITH NO ADVANCING
END-IF
DISPLAY "DOWN AND PASS IT ROUND"
SUBTRACT 1 FROM COUNTER GIVING COUNTER
PERFORM 100-SHOW-NUMBER
DISPLAY " OF BEER ON THE WALL"
ADD 1 TO COUNTER GIVING COUNTER
DISPLAY SPACE
END-PERFORM.

STOP RUN.

100-SHOW-NUMBER.
IF COUNTER = 0
DISPLAY "NO" WITH NO ADVANCING
ELSE
IF COUNTER < 20
DISPLAY FUNCTION TRIM( ADIGITS( COUNTER ) ) WITH NO ADVANCING
ELSE
IF COUNTER < 100
MOVE SPACES TO NUMBER-NAME
STRING ATENS( TENS ) DELIMITED BY SPACE, SPACE DELIMITED BY SIZE,
ADIGITS( DIGITS ) DELIMITED BY SPACE INTO NUMBER-NAME
DISPLAY FUNCTION TRIM( NUMBER-NAME) WITH NO ADVANCING
END-IF
END-IF
END-IF.
IF COUNTER = 1
DISPLAY " BOTTLE" WITH NO ADVANCING
ELSE
DISPLAY " BOTTLES" WITH NO ADVANCING
END-IF.

The format has probably been clobbered by posting here, and the Upper Case 
(shouting) looks unattractive to modern eyes, but the code itself should do 
the job. If it is going to represent COBOL in a public forum then it should 
look "attractive" :-) This is very subjective and certainly arguable, but 
I'll try and show what I mean. The idea is to make it easily readable 
(formatting helps) and to try and eliminate "ugly" constructs and redundant 
code.

I'm going to reformat it and add a few subtle changes... in lower case so 
they show up.

IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
PROGRAM-ID. NINETY-NINE. *> this name may be illegal on some COBOL 
implemenations.
AUTHOR. BRUCE AXTENS with some mods by Pete Dashwood.
ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
DATA DIVISION.
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
01 COUNTER-DIGITS .
      12 tens  pic 9.
      12 digits pic 9.
01 COUNTER REDEFINES COUNTER-DIGITS  pic 99.

           88 TWO-BOTTLES-LEFT VALUE 2.
           88 ONE-BOTTLE-LEFT VALUE 1.
           88 NO-BOTTLES-LEFT VALUE 0.


01 AFTER-TEN-WORDS.
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE SPACES.
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "TWENTY".
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "THIRTY".
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "FORTY".
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "FIFTY".
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "SIXTY".
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "SEVENTY".
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "EIGHTY".
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "NINETY".
    05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE SPACES.

01 AFTER-TEN-ARRAY REDEFINES AFTER-TEN-WORDS.
    05 ATENS OCCURS 10 TIMES PIC X(7) indexed by atens-x1.

01 DIGIT-WORDS.
     05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "ONE".
     05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TWO".
     05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "THREE".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FOUR".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FIVE".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SIX".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SEVEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "EIGHT".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "NINE".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "ELEVEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TWELVE".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "THIRTEEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FOURTEEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FIFTEEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SIXTEEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SEVENTEEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "EIGHTEEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "NINETEEN".
    05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE SPACES.

 01 DIGIT-ARRAY REDEFINES DIGIT-WORDS.
     05 ADIGITS OCCURS 20 TIMES PIC X(9) indexed by adigits-x1.
 01 NUMBER-NAME PIC X(15).
*>  Some stuff added by Pete
*>
 01  display-line pic x(120) value spaces.
*> some useful literals...
 01   BOB           pic x(24) value " BOTTLES OF BEER        ".
 01   BOB-1       pic x(24) value "ONLY ONE BOTTLE OF BEER ".
 01  OTW          pic x(15) value " ON THE WALL...".
 01   TODAPIA pic x(32) value "TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND".
 01   bob-len       pic s9(4) comp  value 17.
PROCEDURE DIVISION.
100-MAIN SECTION.
100-SETUP.
*>  OK, if we are going for a sectioned approach I'll use that.
*>  It seems to me that this "DISPLAY WITH NO ADVANCING" is repetitive, 
clumsy and ugly.
*> Mixing it with lines that DO advance is just primitive...
*>  Why not set up the line you want and just display it?
     PERFORM
          VARYING COUNTER
                FROM 99
                      BY -1
                UNTIL NO-BOTTLES-LEFT
                      perform 100-do-count-lines
                      perform 100-do-remaining-lines
     end-perform
     stop run
    .
*>===========   PERFORMed subroutines here =======
*>  Note: ONLY SECTIONs are PERFORMed out of line in this program.
*>
*>  displays the count of bottles currently on the wall...
 100-do-count-lines section.
 100-dcl-000.
       move spaces to display-line
       perform 110-get-number
       string
             NUMBER-NAME
                   delimited by space
             BOB (1: boblen)
                   delimited by size
             OTW
                    delimited by size
                        into display-line
       end-string
       display display-line
       move spaces to display-line
       string
             NUMBER-NAME
                   delimited by space
             BOB (1:bob-len)
                   delimited by size
                        into display-line
       end-string
       display display-line
       display TODAPIA
       .
 100-dcl-000.
        exit.
*>---------------------------------------------------------------------------
*>  displays the count of bottles remaining after one is removed
 100-do-remaining-lines section.
 100-drl-000.
       move spaces to display-line
       compute COUNTER = COUNTER - 1
       perform 110-get-number
       string
             NUMBER-NAME
                   delimited by space
             BOB (1:bob-len)
                   delimited by size
             OTW
                    delimited by size
                        into display-line
       end-string
       display display-line
       move spaces to display-line
       display display-line
       compute COUNTER = COUNTER + 1
       .
  100-drl-999.
        exit.
*>------------------------------------------------------------------------------
  110-get-number section.
  110-gn-000.
        if tens NOT = zero
            set atens-x1 to tens
        end-if
        evaluate TRUE
            when COUNTER < 1
                  move spaces to NUMBER-NAME
                  move "NO MORE BOTTLES OF BEER " to BOB
                  move "GO GET SOME MORE BOTTLES OF BEER..."  to TODAPIA
            when COUNTER < 2
                  move spaces to NUMBER-NAME
                  move BOB-1 to BOB
                  move 24 to bob-len
                 move "TAKE IT DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND" to TODAPIA
            when COUNTER < 20
                  set adigits-x1 to counter
                  move ADIGITS (adigits-x1) to NUMBER-NAME
            when OTHER
                  move ATENS (atens-x1) to NUMBER-NAME
                  if digits not = 0
                     set adigits-x1 to digits
                     string
                                  NUMBER-NAME
                                        delimited by space
                                  "-"
                                        delimited by size
                                   ADIGITS (adigits-x1)
                                        delimited by space
                                             into NUMBER-NAME
                      end-string
                   end-if
        end-evaluate.
        .
   110-gn-999.
         exit.

Although this takes more lines because it uses white space to improve 
readability, it removes the need for the TRIM function and the multiple 
DISPLAYS with and without ADVANCING.

I don't have Open COBOL so I have no idea whether it will compile on that 
(perhaps someone could let me know?) but it DOES compile and run on Fujitsu 
NetCOBOL Version 9. Although it compiled (and ran) OK, the name COUNTER is a 
reserved word in some COBOLs so I changed it to ITERATION-COUNT.

Finally, I converted the whole program into UPPER case, to bring it back in 
line with the original, and here it is, hopefully in a form that will cut 
and paste easily into a COBOL Editor...

       IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
       PROGRAM-ID. NINETY-NINE.
            AUTHOR. BRUCE AXTENS WITH SOME MODS BY PETE DASHWOOD.
       ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
       DATA DIVISION.
       WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
       01 ITERATION-COUNT-DIGITS.
           12 TENS   PIC 9.
           12 DIGITS PIC 9.
       01 ITERATION-COUNT REDEFINES ITERATION-COUNT-DIGITS  PIC 99.

           88 TWO-BOTTLES-LEFT VALUE 2.
           88 ONE-BOTTLE-LEFT VALUE 1.
           88 NO-BOTTLES-LEFT VALUE 0.

       01  AFTER-TEN-WORDS.
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE SPACES.
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "TWENTY".
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "THIRTY".
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "FORTY".
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "FIFTY".
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "SIXTY".
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "SEVENTY".
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "EIGHTY".
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE "NINETY".
           05 FILLER PIC X(7) VALUE SPACES.

       01  AFTER-TEN-ARRAY REDEFINES AFTER-TEN-WORDS.
           05 ATENS PIC X(7) OCCURS 10
                             INDEXED BY ATENS-X1.

       01  DIGIT-WORDS.
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "ONE".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TWO".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "THREE".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FOUR".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FIVE".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SIX".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SEVEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "EIGHT".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "NINE".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "ELEVEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "TWELVE".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "THIRTEEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FOURTEEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "FIFTEEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SIXTEEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "SEVENTEEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "EIGHTEEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE "NINETEEN".
           05 FILLER PIC X(9) VALUE SPACES.

       01  DIGIT-ARRAY REDEFINES DIGIT-WORDS.
           05 ADIGITS  PIC X(9) OCCURS 20 TIMES
                                INDEXED BY ADIGITS-X1.
       01  NUMBER-NAME PIC X(15).
      *>  SOME STUFF ADDED BY PETE
      *>
       01  DISPLAY-LINE PIC X(120) VALUE SPACES.
       01  BOB     PIC X(23) VALUE " BOTTLES OF BEER       ".
       01  BOB-1   PIC X(23) VALUE "ONLY ONE BOTTLE OF BEER".
       01  OTW     PIC X(15) VALUE " ON THE WALL...".
       01  TODAPIA PIC X(32) VALUE "TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND".
       01  BOB-LEN PIC S9(4) COMP  VALUE 17.

       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
       100-MAIN SECTION.
       100-SETUP.
           PERFORM
                VARYING ITERATION-COUNT
                      FROM 99
                            BY -1
                      UNTIL NO-BOTTLES-LEFT
                            PERFORM 100-DO-COUNT-LINES
                            PERFORM 100-DO-REMAINING-LINES
           END-PERFORM
           STOP RUN
           .
      *>===========   PERFORMED SUBROUTINES HERE ======================
      *>  NOTE: ONLY SECTIONS ARE PERFORMED OUT OF LINE IN THIS PROGRAM,
      *>        AND THEY ARE NEVER DROPPED INTO OR OUT OF.
      *>  DISPLAYS THE COUNT OF BOTTLES CURRENTLY ON THE WALL...
       100-DO-COUNT-LINES SECTION.
       100-DCL-000.
             MOVE SPACES TO DISPLAY-LINE
             PERFORM 110-GET-NUMBER
             STRING
                   NUMBER-NAME
                         DELIMITED BY SPACE
                   BOB (1: BOB-LEN)
                         DELIMITED BY SIZE
                   OTW
                          DELIMITED BY SIZE
                              INTO DISPLAY-LINE
             END-STRING
             DISPLAY DISPLAY-LINE
             MOVE SPACES TO DISPLAY-LINE
             STRING
                   NUMBER-NAME
                         DELIMITED BY SPACE
                   BOB (1:BOB-LEN)
                         DELIMITED BY SIZE
                              INTO DISPLAY-LINE
             END-STRING
             DISPLAY DISPLAY-LINE
             DISPLAY TODAPIA
             .
       100-DCL-000.
              EXIT.
      *>---------------------------------------------------------------
      *>  DISPLAYS THE COUNT OF BOTTLES REMAINING AFTER ONE IS REMOVED
       100-DO-REMAINING-LINES SECTION.
       100-DRL-000.
             MOVE SPACES TO DISPLAY-LINE
             COMPUTE ITERATION-COUNT = ITERATION-COUNT - 1
             PERFORM 110-GET-NUMBER
             STRING
                   NUMBER-NAME
                         DELIMITED BY SPACE
                   BOB (1:BOB-LEN)
                         DELIMITED BY SIZE
                   OTW
                          DELIMITED BY SIZE
                              INTO DISPLAY-LINE
             END-STRING
             DISPLAY DISPLAY-LINE
             MOVE SPACES TO DISPLAY-LINE
             DISPLAY DISPLAY-LINE
             COMPUTE ITERATION-COUNT = ITERATION-COUNT + 1
             .
        100-DRL-999.
              EXIT.
       *>------------------------------------------------------------------------------
       * THIS SUBROUTINE SETS UP LINES FOR DISPLAY AND TRANSLATES THE
       * ITERATION-COUNT INTO WORDS.
       *
        110-GET-NUMBER SECTION.
        110-GN-000.
              IF TENS NOT = ZERO
                  SET ATENS-X1 TO TENS
              END-IF
              EVALUATE TRUE
                  WHEN ITERATION-COUNT < 1
                        MOVE SPACES TO NUMBER-NAME
                        MOVE "NO MORE BOTTLES OF BEER " TO BOB
                        MOVE "GO GET SOME MORE BOTTLES OF BEER..."  TO 
TODAPIA
                  WHEN ITERATION-COUNT < 2
                        MOVE SPACES TO NUMBER-NAME
                        MOVE BOB-1 TO BOB
                        MOVE 23 TO BOB-LEN
                       MOVE "TAKE IT DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND" TO TODAPIA
                  WHEN ITERATION-COUNT < 20
                        SET ADIGITS-X1 TO ITERATION-COUNT
                        MOVE ADIGITS (ADIGITS-X1) TO NUMBER-NAME
                  WHEN OTHER
                        MOVE ATENS (ATENS-X1) TO NUMBER-NAME
                        IF DIGITS NOT = 0
                           SET ADIGITS-X1 TO DIGITS
                           STRING
                                        NUMBER-NAME
                                              DELIMITED BY SPACE
                                        "-"
                                              DELIMITED BY SIZE
                                         ADIGITS (ADIGITS-X1)
                                              DELIMITED BY SPACE
                                                   INTO NUMBER-NAME
                            END-STRING
                         END-IF
              END-EVALUATE.
              .
         110-GN-999.
               EXIT.


Here's some sample output...


NINETY-NINE BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...

TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND
NINETY-EIGHT BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...

NINETY-EIGHT BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...

TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND
NINETY-SEVEN BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...
...

NINETY-ONE BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...
NINETY-ONE BOTTLES OF BEER
TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND
NINETY BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...

NINETY BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...
NINETY BOTTLES OF BEER
TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND
EIGHTY-NINE BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...
...

TWENTY-ONE BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...
TWENTY-ONE BOTTLES OF BEER
TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND
TWENTY BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...

TWENTY BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...
TWENTY BOTTLES OF BEER
TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND
NINETEEN BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...

NINETEEN BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...
NINETEEN BOTTLES OF BEER
TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND
EIGHTEEN BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...

EIGHTEEN BOTTLES OF BEER  ON THE WALL...
EIGHTEEN BOTTLES OF BEER
TAKE ONE DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND

...

ONLY ONE BOTTLE OF BEER
TAKE IT DOWN AND PASS IT AROUND
NO MORE BOTTLES OF BEER ON THE WALL...

It seems to me that this should really be written as a recursive program, 
but standard COBOL doesn't lend itself to that easily.

Perhaps a follow-up to Rosetta Code could show the same application written 
recursively in OO COBOL or C# ?

Pete.

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Alistair Maclean wrote:
> 
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

Whoa there - no need to explore to establish what's going on. It works 
fine in N/E V 3.1, working in DEBUG mode (which is all Alistair should 
really need to do), I got the DOS display box. So Alistair, not quite 
sure what's going on, but from the IDE, select 'View' and then 
'Application Output' - it should do the trick with an Icon for 
Application Output in the Taskbar.

Ok it whizzes through the display before you get a chance to read it.
Picked this up from Jerome Garfunkel who 'wrote' a Calendar using Date 
Routines. Output is "DISPLAY this etc.....", but, starting in Column 7 :-

$ set OutDD"Calendar.txt 80 L A"

Translates "Displays" to "Writes" to a LineSequential. Can't remember 
full syntax because I've forgotten as it doesn't work with OO classes.
Add the above '$ set' as the first line and recompile and it should go 
to the file name you specify.

Yes I'm looking at an OO example - will only post if I think it has 
merit over and above what you did. (Well your 'Beloved' Sections would 
become 'Methods' ).
```

Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> I did consider posting an OO version but thought I'd leave it to someone 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

You mentioned C# - take a look at this - can you improve ? Mind you,
according to the initial simple rules for '99 Bottles' - it's limited
compared to your COBOL Procedural example :-

	http://rosettacode.org/wiki/99_Bottles_of_Beer,

Or this, which appears to be C# in its entirety (?) , or, not knowing 
the language, does it need the Preprocessor code immediately above this 
example ?

---------------------------------------------------------------------
http://rosettacode.org/wiki/99_Bottles_of_Beer/C%2B%2B/Object_Oriented
----------------------------------------------------------------------
using System;

class Program
{
     static void Main(string[] args)
     {
         for (int i = 99; i > -1; i--)
         {
             if (i == 0)
             {
                 Console.WriteLine("No more bottles of beer on the wall, 
no more bottles of beer.");
                 Console.WriteLine("Go to the store and buy some more, 
99 bottles of beer on the wall.");
                 break;
             }
             if (i == 1)
             {
                 Console.WriteLine("1 bottle of beer on the wall, 1 
bottle of beer.");
                 Console.WriteLine("Take one down and pass it around, no 
more bottles of beer on the wall.");
                 Console.WriteLine();
             }
             else
             {
                 Console.WriteLine("{0} bottles of beer on the wall, {0} 
bottles of beer.", i);
                 Console.WriteLine("Take one down and pass it around, 
{0} bottles of beer on the wall.", i - 1);
                 Console.WriteLine();
             }
         }
     }
}
[edit]
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Don't go looking Sunday, October 17th as they are revamping the site; 
possibly they will remove some of the confusion they have on the 'C' 
group - you apparently have to use 'C Sharp' instead of 'C#", to search.

```
James Gavan wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[61 more quoted lines elided]…
> search.

I did look at these a few days ago.

The C# example (there are several, but I'm referring to the one you posted 
above) "cheats" in that it doesn't convert the numbers to words (and so 
doesn't require recursion). Partly because of this, it solves the problem in 
about 25 lines of code. I considered extending it to provide words, but then 
Reality bit and I decided my time might be better applied elsewhere.

Sometimes I need a break so I come here, but I really can't spend a lot of 
time on what has to be a pointless exercise, as far as I am concerned. I'll 
explain more below.

I thought there might be a .Net Class that converts digits to words but, as 
far as I can tell, there isn't. The code to do it is considered to be so 
trivial there was no need. (I suppose, by the time you consider doing it for 
120 odd National languages they decided not to :-))

To me, the most interesting (and the shortest) example on the Rosetta Code 
"Bottles of Beer" pages was the C# example using Linq. This is a total 
"cheat" in that it requires a database containing the 99 entries, but it 
does solve the whole thing in a few lines of C# and it does give some 
insight into Linq as a replacement for embedded SQL.

I spent time on the COBOL example because I still have a soft spot for COBOL 
and it just didn't seem right for all those ghastly DISPLAYS to be 
representing the language in a programming forum. However, the OP's example 
would certainly work and there are people who would consider the code I 
posted to be "ugly", so a lot of it is about style.

These days, I am writing mainly C# and trying to extend my knowledge of it. 
(There is a lot to learn once the basics are mastered, Reflection, 
Delegation, Linq, Lambdas, the new multi-processing features, and so on... 
you can go as far as you want and it is all very interesting. )  The 
Migration Toolset is written mainly in C# although it still uses a few OO 
COBOL components. Newer tools from PRIMA are entirely in C#.

I'm currently writing a tool for a client which should be quite useful to 
people who develop and market software. The Client has built (over 25 years) 
a good customer base for a niche package which he usually sells. He has 
decided (wisely, in my opinion) to stop selling it and lease it  to people 
instead. The problem is that some of his clients will probably not pay their 
bills, but will continue to enjoy the software :-). He has commissioned a 
tool (it will be 2 components) that will "phone home" to his web server and 
check the client status, whenever the package is started (if the Internet is 
available, obviously). Depending on the status the package receives, it will 
start itself in full availability, less availability, Demo mode, or not at 
all. The 2 components are a Web Service and a component that will be 
embedded in his package. The package is written in COBOL, but the Remote 
Application Validation will be in C#. As it is component based, I expect the 
two languages to play together nicely. :-)

The Web Service is working and accesses a PostgreSQL DB on his web server to 
get the client status. The Web Service has been written to support Access, 
SQL Server, or PostgreSQL, so future customers for this tool have a choice.

We are still looking at what the second component will do. It obviously 
switches to a "Startup" depending on the status it gets, but there is a lot 
more that it COULD do. The second component will be provided as a COM 
component that is guaranteed to work with COBOL (or anything else that 
supports the Component Object Model.)

If anyone here is developing software for sale and is interested in 
protecting it from unauthorised use, on an ongoing basis, please mail me 
privately.

Using VS 2008 and C#, the Web Service was really simple and easy to 
implement. The second, "embedded", component is mainly about properties of 
the component, so it could be implemented easily in either OO COBOL or C#. 
(I'll use C# for consistency and speed of writing, but the source language 
really doesn't matter.)

People using a GUI development IDE (like Fujitsu PowerCOBOL...) will see the 
component appear in their Toolbox and it will reveal its methods and 
properties just like any other control, and can be dragged and dropped onto 
a form just like any other control. For VB, NetCOBOL, and C/C++ it gets 
added as a project reference (in Eclipse or Visual Studio) and then all it's 
properties/methods are available through Intellisense.

The days when source code was King and everything revolved around having 
source, are gone, at least in my world. People buy stuff and expect it to 
work (I do too...). They are not interested in changing it or fiddling with 
the mechanics of it. Functionality is what matters. I produce components 
that run with any language supporting the Component model. People use them 
with COBOL, VB, C# and C++. If they are not entirely satisfied or want some 
minor changes, I do them for free; if they are not happy I give them a 
refund. (That has never happened yet...:-))

I have no regrets about moving away from the monolithic model suggested by 
COBOL, where programs do everything within themselves. It makes sense to me 
to use layered (tiered) levels of functionality (Presentation, Business 
logic, Data access, as a minimum...) and if these layers can be encapsulated 
into entirely separate objects that can be deployed anywhere (Desktop or Web 
based) and re-used over and over, that HAS to be a cost saving. (This 
approach does require some careful thought about interfaces, but that is 
beyond the scope of what I'm writing here. The idea is to keep interfaces 
consistent so that processes only see an interface and what is behind it can 
be changed without everything falling apart.)

It certainly makes maintenance much easier, irrespective of the language you 
write it in. Small pieces of code are easier to assimilate and fix than 
large pieces. Small pieces of code get loaded faster and can be handled by 
the system with much more flexibility than large pieces. The only time I can 
see where monolithic is good, is in a batch process where it can be loaded 
once and then just keeps on going on until it finishes. (Probably hogging 
all the resources and degrading everything else in the process... :-))

But the modern world is mostly about networks and interactive applications.

Modular programming is great, Objects implement it better, and OO Components 
are about as good as it gets... :-)

Pete.
```

Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Oct 15, 3:14 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
<snip>>
> Finally got it working. It was the fact that I had pasted the code in

> compiler gave the impression of running correctly (no error messages)
> but clearly didn't work.

Good, glad you got it working.
>
> Output is ok but tedious to page through.

Hmmm...

1. Open a DOS box. (Start>run> cmd  OR  Start>All 
programs>Acessories>command prompt)
2. Maximise it. (With the gadget in the top right hand corner of the window)
3. Move to the directory where the executable is:  cd  \bottlesofbeer [or 
whatever its path is on your machine]
4. Run the program and pipe its its output to MORE:  ninety-nine | more

It should be done in about 3 pages... hardly "tedious", but you cannot page 
back up...

In order to get output that you CAN page up and down you need to pipe the 
output to a text file that you can look at with notepad or Word (Where the 
page Up and Page down keys are implemented. Again, make sure you look at it 
in a maximised window.)

replace step 4 with:

4 Run the program and redirect its output to a text file: ninety-nine > 
bob.txt
5. Look at the text file in Notepad: notepad bob.txt

If you really think it is tedious, why not amend the code so it can accept a 
command line parameter that tells it where to start from? (The program 
checks for a parameter and if it finds a valid number (0 -99) it sets 
ITERATION-COUNT to that in the main PERFORM. If there is no parameter, 
default it to 99.

It's only COBOL... :-)

Pete.
```

> with it?

It's sorta there, but it's not a "DOS window." DOS was completely done away 
with in WinXP and does not exist, in any form, in XP, Vista, or Win7.

What you have instead is a "command interpreter" which emulates a DOS 
environment.

So, to answer your question without telling you more than you want to know: 
Yes, the DOS window is still there.

In my WinXP there are two command interpreters found in 
c:\windows\system32:  command.com (which emulates 16-bit DOS) and 
cmd.exe (which is 32-bit for windows NT and OS/2).

Windows 7 still has cmd.exe, but you'll have to create your own shortcut 
to it.  I suspect that command.com may not exist for Vista or Windows 7.

There's a freeware DOS emulator available that runs in WinXP, Vista, and 
Windows 7 (32-bit or 64-bit).  It's also been ported to Mac, Linux, and 
FreeBSD:
http://www.dosbox.com/

Arnold Trembley wrote:
> On 10/16/2010 6:56 PM, HeyBub wrote:
>> Alistair Maclean wrote:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> shortcut to it.

It is still in the "All programs> Accessories" menu and you can simply drag 
and drop it to the desktop.

>  I suspect that command.com may not exist for Vista
> or Windows 7.

It certainly isn't in my version of Windows 7 :-)


>
> There's a freeware DOS emulator available that runs in WinXP, Vista,
> and Windows 7 (32-bit or 64-bit).  It's also been ported to Mac,
> Linux, and FreeBSD:
> http://www.dosbox.com/

Speasking only for myself, I don't want to run DOS on Windows 7; however, I 
am happy to have a command line interface.

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<H8-dnbWyN55GgynRnZ2dnUVZ7sidnZ2d@westnet.com.au> <43da28e5-a68b-4ffd-8484-701b2c4fc601@j25g2000yqa.googlegroups.com> <i9f818$nnc$1@reader1.panix.com> <04624f26-ff62-41cf-9036-20e1ad9811cc@p26g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <i9hbha$p1i$1@reader1.panix.com> <%bMvo.317483$Yn5.207419@en-nntp-14.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
On Oct 21, 1:55 pm, "Bill Klein" <wmkl...@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
wrote:
> <docdw...@panix.com> wrote in messagenews:i9hbha$p1i$1@reader1.panix.com...
> > In article
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> was ever possible via the commands that I tried to use.

NULL: is a 'device', the colon is optional. It is a knock off of the
Unix/Linux /dev/nul. These can be used to discard output as in
'command >NULL:'. If you try deleting it with 'delete null' then the
system will try to delete the device.

Have you tried using the complete path name, as in: 'delete "C:/
Documents and Folders/All Users/UserBill/Documents and Folders/A few
more layers/some folder/null"' ?
```

Richard wrote:

…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> system will try to delete the device.

The device is no longer NULL: (If memory serves, it was, on DOS...) On win 7 
it is NUL (with or without the colon)
There is no such command as "delete" on Win 7 (I don't remember there ever 
being on DOS either, but I could be wrong about that.)
The correct command for Win 7 is DEL. (And this has always worked in DOS).

>
> Have you tried using the complete path name, as in: 'delete "C:/
> Documents and Folders/All Users/UserBill/Documents and Folders/A few
> more layers/some folder/null"' ?

That's a good idea.  But, as noted, "Delete" can't work and if DEL can 
reference the file through a path, it should reference it OK if the path 
happens to be the current directory.

I'm thinking Bill's file has been locked or has had some attribute set that 
is making it read only.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: DOS Window / Command (was: Before I submit this to RosettaCode ...

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-22T00:17:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8ialr7F655U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<H8-dnbWyN55GgynRnZ2dnUVZ7sidnZ2d@westnet.com.au> <43da28e5-a68b-4ffd-8484-701b2c4fc601@j25g2000yqa.googlegroups.com> <i9f818$nnc$1@reader1.panix.com> <04624f26-ff62-41cf-9036-20e1ad9811cc@p26g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <i9hbha$p1i$1@reader1.panix.com> <%bMvo.317483$Yn5.207419@en-nntp-14.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Bill Klein wrote:
> <docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message
> news:i9hbha$p1i$1@reader1.panix.com...
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> this was ever possible via the commands that I tried to use.
 Bill,

this sounds like someone tried to redirect something to the device NULL. (It 
should be NUL:)

If the file that won't go away is called "nul" then something seriously 
wrong happened :-). Redirection to "null" or "null:" creates a file called 
"null" which is easily removed.

On WinXP redirection to "nul" or "nul:" does NOT cause a problem and it 
doesn't create a file, it just "burns" the output. (I just tried it to 
confirm this...)

I ran exactly the same tests on Win 7 and it does exactly the same.

So, if you have a file called "null" or "nul" (the latter appears to be 
impossible; I tried everything I could think of in Win 7 to create one 
without success) and the standard DEL (NOT "Delete") does not remove it it 
has to be because the file is protected.

Try this:

(On Win 7)

1. Open the command prompt and CD to the path where your file is. (Let's say 
the file is called "null"...)
2. Type the following at the prompt: ATTRIB NULL -R -A -S -H
3. press enter.
4. type the following: DEL nul*.*   (there is a risk here, but you said it 
was the only file in the directory... if that's right there is no risk.)
5. Press enter.

It should be gone. Try: DIR nul*.*  to see.

If it is still there it is because it has been locked by some long forgotten 
process (or a running system process) and you will need a tool like 
"unlocker" to remove it.

If the file is actually called "nul", amend the lines above as follows:

2. Type the following at the prompt: ATTRIB NUL -R -A -S -H
4. type the following: DEL nu*.*   (there is a risk here, but you said it 
was the only file in the directory... if that's right there is no risk.)

HTH,

Pete.
  -- 
"I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

Just like all of us, Alistair, you are entitled to feel however you want 
about this.

Personally, I think you are doing Win XP an injustice, but I also accept 
that you have a perfect right to... :-)

(Win XP, for me, was the FIRST Microsoft OS I was totally happy with. I 
think there are millions who feel the same way and I am really glad I never 
jumped into Vista. Win 7, based on limited samplng by me, looks excellent... 
even without a DOS box... :-))

Everyone's experience is subjective. I never had problems with DOS boxes in 
DOS or ANY version of Windows, but you obviously did.

I have never had a Windows system refuse to boot into safe mode, but I'm 
sure there are people who have. Just as there are people who rebuild their 
entire system if they get a virus infection, (when removal is pretty easy 
once you understand how these things work) or reformat their hard drive 
because the local computer shop couldn't fix their problem (or told them it 
was necessary), or emasculate their Browser Experience because the Internet 
is dangerous... and so on.

All I will say on this is that if you think Win 95 was the greatest and 
would revert to that, surely, you would use Win 98? I'm talking Win 98 SE 
not ME... Millennium Edition was just unadulterated crap and I wouldn't 
install my stuff on machines running it. (I lost a couple of sales at the 
time but I counted myself lucky; I saw other guys tearing their hair out 
because software that had functioned perfectly on Win 95 fell apart on Win 
98 ME.) Win 98 SE (Second Edition) addressed the problems and saved the day.

If and when the time comes and you move to Win 7 (or whatever else) I hope 
the experience is less troubling for you.

Incidentally, did you know that "XP" means "experience"?

Pete.
```

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
>
> I first got Windows when it was 3.1. Then Win 95, then both variants
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Registry cleaners are ALL snake oil. There is NOTHING that can be done to a 
registry that will slow a working computer or use an inordinate amount of 
disk space. The registry is searched - when needed - as an optimized 
relational database, NOT SEQUENTIALLY. Whether the registry has a few 
hundred entries or several million results in an almost non-detectable 
difference in elapsed time.

The BEST that can be said, pragmatically, of ANY registry cleaner is that it 
will probably do no harm. Regrettably, many have the capacity (and, 
seemingly, the desire) to corrupt your system leading to malfunction or 
total non-function.

We have discovered one case in which a registry cleaner is called for. This 
situation arises when the operator is afflicted with an obsessive-compulsive 
disorder that demands daily registry cleaning and dusting the inside of 
shelved books.

To answer your question, the technical newsgroups that I frequent report 
that CCLEANER at least does no harm.

Yet.

http://www.piriform.com/ccleaner
```

Although I would agree in principle with most of what HeyBub wrote, there 
CAN be performance problems when Registry hives become seriously fractured. 
I use a cleaner (Uniblue Registry Booster) which can clean AND defrag the 
Registry. I run it about 3 times a year if I think performance is becoming 
noticeably slower. It USUALLY (but not always...) improves things.

It has never harmed my system and there have been a couple of occasions when 
I was glad of its ability to restore registry entries if needed.

It isn't free. (What's having an optimized Registry worth? Nothing...? Not 
to me...)

On Fri, 22 Oct 2010 13:57:22 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Alistair Maclean wrote:
>> On Oct 19, 9:19 pm, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
…[59 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pete.

I'm with Pete on this.  On both my Win98 and WinXP boxes, I've seen
the registry get fragmented and cause problems.  On the WinXP box, the
entries for MS Excel and Word became so corrupted that I had to
uninstall both products, manually delete all references in the
registry to both and then reinstall the products.  

I now have System Mechanics installed on both boxes and regularly have
it scan, defrag and eliminate unused registry entries.  I've not
experienced any problems with this product and haven't had any
registry issues either. 

The registry may not be a huge file but it is central to much of
Windows' processing.  Ignoring it just doesn't make sense.

Regards,

Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Oct 17, 5:25 pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
>> In article
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> use it any more.

This caught my attention... :-)

If there is "little that I can do in the window" (apart from navigating, 
which Windows explorer can do more intuitively) then I can't imagine how you 
would "have occasion to use it"....?

In actuality, there are a good number of commands that are supported by the 
command processor and, in the Oldene Dayze, may of us were thankful for 
them.Certainly, many people also never got past "CD", "DIR" and "DEL"...

But time (and computer software) move on. All of the commands that were 
available under DOS are now replaced by better, modern equivalents in 
Windows Script Hosting, which have far more richness and capability. They 
have also added full File System support which DOS never really had.WSH is a 
complete language with sequence, selection, and iteration. And , of course, 
it also supports objects... A long way from DOS. (Actually, it is better 
than JCL on mainframes...; I'd equate it with REXX for that environment.)

Pete.

Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Oct 17, 12:56 am, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
>> Alistair Maclean wrote:

That is not the case at all.

DOS just isn't relevant.

You can start programs from a command line and it is similar to doing it 
with DOS but you can do that on a Mac or almost any other computer. There is 
a world of difference between a CLI and PC DOS. If you used to enjoy writing 
DOS batch files (as I did) now you can write WSH scripts (actually, you've 
been able to do this on Win XP for years so you've had plenty of time to 
learn how) which are many times more powerful than DOS batch and not "dumbed 
down" in any way. In fact, removing DOS, far from "dumbing things down" is 
empowering for people who are prepared to embrace change.

(Those who are not, can  still run their old DOS batch files, as the CLI 
recognises .bat and executes it (I just wrote a quick batch file to test 
it). Doing this is like driving your Ferrari as if it were a horse...)

I guess Microsoft are more understanding than I would be. If it were down to 
me I wouldn't let it execute .bat; I'd offer a converter that would build 
script files from .bat.

Sometimes people need to be dragged kicking and screaming into a better 
world... :-)

Pete.
