```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Oct 17, 12:56 am, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
>> Alistair Maclean wrote:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> with a poor dumbed-down substitute".

You interpret incorrectly. The Command Interpreter in XP and above is a DOS 
prompt on steroids.

Just taking the letter "A" as an example:

ARP: Displays and modifies entries in the Address Resolution Protocol (ARP) 
cache, which contains one or more tables that are used to store IP addresses 
and their resolved Ethernet or Token Ring physical addresses.

ASSOC: Displays or modifies file name extension associations.

AT: Schedules commands and programs to run on a computer at a specified time 
and date.

ATMADM: Monitors connections and addresses that are registered by the ATM 
Call Manager on an asynchronous transfer mode (ATM) network.

ATTRIB: Displays, sets, or removes the read-only, archive, system, and 
hidden attributes assigned to files or directories.

Command Changed features
chcp
diskcomp
diskperf
diskcopy
doskey
format
label
mode.
print
prompt
recover
rmdir
sort
xcopy

There are a number of commands that have been deleted, including DOSSHELL, 
FDISK, etc.


New command-line tools

bootcfg -Configures, queries, or changes Boot.ini file settings.
defrag - Locates and consolidates fragmented boot files, data files, and 
folders on local volumes.
diskpart - Manages disks, partitions, or volumes.
driverquery - Queries for a list of drivers and driver properties.
eventcreate - Enables an administrator to create a custom event in a 
specified event log.
eventquery - Lists the events and event properties from one or more event 
logs.
eventtriggers - Displays and configures event triggers on local or remote 
machines.
fsutil - Manages reparse points, managing sparse files, dismounting a 
volume, or extending a volume.
getmac - Obtains the media access control (MAC) address and list of network 
protocols
helpctr - Starts Help and Support Center.
ipseccmd - Configures Internet Protocol Security (IPSec) policies in the 
directory service, or in a local or remote registry.
Ipseccmd  - is a command-line version of the IP Security Policies Microsoft 
Management Console (MMC) snap-in.
logman - Manages and schedules performance counter and event trace log 
collections on local and remote systems.
openfiles - Queries, displays, or disconnects open files.
pagefileconfig - Displays and configures the paging file Virtual Memory 
settings of a system.
perfmon - Enables you to open a Performance console configured with settings 
files from Windows NT 4.0 version of Performance Monitor.
prncnfg - Configures or displays configuration information about a printer.
prndrvr - Adds, deletes, and lists printer drivers from local or remote 
print servers.
prnjobs - Pauses, resumes, cancels, and lists print jobs.
prnmngr - Adds, deletes, and lists printers or printer connections, in 
addition to setting and displaying the default printer.
prnport - Creates, deletes, and lists standard TCP/IP printer ports, in 
addition to displaying and changing port configuration.
prnqctl - Prints a test page, pauses or resumes a printer, and clears a 
printer queue.
relog - Extracts performance counters from performance counter logs into 
other formats, such as text-TSV (for tab-delimited text), text-CSV (for 
comma-delimited text), binary-BIN, or SQL.
sc - Retrieves and sets information about services. Tests and debugs service 
programs.
schtasks - Schedules commands and programs to run periodically or at a 
specific time. Adds and removes tasks from the schedule, starts and stops 
tasks on demand, and displays and changes scheduled tasks.
shutdown - Shuts down or restarts a local or remote computer.
systeminfo - Queries the system for basic system configuration information.
taskkill - Ends one or more tasks or processes.
tasklist - Displays a list of applications, services, and the Process ID 
(PID) currently running on either a local or a remote computer.
tracerpt - Processes event trace logs or real-time data from instrumented 
event trace providers and allows you to generate trace analysis reports and 
CSV (comma-delimited) files for the events generated.
typeperf - Writes performance counter data to the command window or to a 
supported log file format.
WMIC - Eases the use of Windows Management Insturmentation (WMI) and systems 
managed through WMI.
```

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Oct 16, 3:07 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> through.

LOL!

Why would you hold the Enter key down? :-)

If you pipe it to MORE, it will fill a screen (page) and stop. (I think it 
says"Press any key to continue..." butI can't remember. Anyway, if you press 
Enter (or any other key) it will fill the screen again with the next "page", 
and so on.) This is NOT writing the output to a file; it is sending the 
output from the program directly to a screen utility called MORE, which 
places it (one screenful at a time) on the screen for you. (That's why you 
can't page back...)

If you take the scond option and redirect the output (this is NOT the same 
as piping it as we did with MORE; note that redirection involves a chevron 
(>) while piping involves a vertical bar ( |   pipe?)) the output is 
redirected to the file you have specified. You can then look at the file it 
created with any software that can handle text. The effect of holding down 
Enter will depend on the software you are using but I shouldn't think it 
would be useful with most programs :-).

Note that if you are using a program (like Notepad) which places the file in 
a window, you can then smoothly scroll through the file, (UP and DOWN) using 
the Window scroll bar on the right.


>
> Does the DOS window still happen under Windows 7 or have MS done away
> with it?

A very good question. What you are really asking is how Windows 7 deals with 
standard output, or data sent by a program to STDOUT. Windows 7 still has 
the "Command Prompt" (It probably isn't fair to refer to it as a "DOS box" 
any longer) so I am betting the program will run in that exactly as it does 
under Win XP.)

Unfortunately, I can't actually run it because I don't have COBOL on my win 
7 VM. Putting together a deployment package involves building an .MSI 
installer with Microsoft installer Merge Modules provided by Alchemy, and is 
a complete pain in the arse. It requires a full setup, CAB files and GUID 
encoded .dlls just to provide the runtime. In the good old days, you could 
unZip a self-extracting WinZip .EXE and Bob's your avuncular relative. 
Obviously, making it hard does nothing to encourage people to use COBOL. 
Anyway, I don't plan to sully my new Win 7 Pro VM with Alchemy product 
unless there is a very good reason to; it is primarily for testing that C# 
applications will run under Win XP and Win 7. (So far, they do... And they 
require no special effort to deploy; Click Once does it very nicely :-))

If anyone has actually compiled the program and run it under Win 7 perhaps 
they might report here?

I'm interested to see if it compiles under Open COBOL and if THAT will run 
under Win 7....

Pete.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<H8-dnbWyN55GgynRnZ2dnUVZ7sidnZ2d@westnet.com.au> <128392a6-d399-44f2-a360-163091d5568b@z28g2000yqh.googlegroups.com> <scudnRIRq8zTqCnRnZ2dnUVZ8rqdnZ2d@westnet.com.au> <b700e377-173e-4597-9ccc-de20a69b4b1a@30g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <gVZso.175$iA3.20@viwinnwfe02.internal.bigpond.com> <6aqdnSbStud8NynRnZ2dnUVZ_gydnZ2d@earthlink.com> <8hoq6jF8g5U2@mid.individual.net> <Yqudnb4qB7_qpiDRnZ2dnUVZ7t2dnZ2d@westnet.com.au>`

```
Bruce M. Axtens wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>         IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> for removing double spaces but otherwise nothing of note.

Yes, I was aware of the double spaces after I looked at the output but I was 
too tired to fix it and it didn't really matter to me... (Using a 
proportional font crossed my mind... :-)) The important thing (as far as I 
was concerned) was to show that COBOL can be useful and simple, and because 
it is simple, doesn't mean it has to be messy...

The problem should have had a recursed solution but again, I didn't want to 
make it my life's work.

If you picked up anything from seeing your code amended (other than being 
offended) I am content.

>
> I have already submitted my original with a couple of changes. Trying
> to decide whether to replace mine with yours or to make mine able to
> display the hyphens.

Thank you Bruce, for not taking my response personally. (Some people 
would've, even though it was never intended to be.)

And for letting me know about the fact that the code runs with Open COBOL.

I consider the code to be yours, because the changes I made were really 
stylistic, rather than fixing logic errors. Your original code was logically 
correct; the changes were therefore stylistic. (I removed a couple of 
redundancies, but these were nothing in the scheme of things.)

The hyphens do look better (at least to my eye, but I purport to be a 
writer...:-))

I think the main objections I had were the DISPLAYs... I have never liked 
this verb, ever since the first times I used it in 1967 on IBM golfball 
typewriter consoles. It DOES vary across platforms with different defaults, 
there is confusion when it is applied to SCREEN SECTIONS, and
 in my book is "best avoided". The days when it was necessary to provide 
"low volume information" to an Operator console are pretty much over, I 
reckon. Want to tell the guy something? Use a message box. Want a record of 
why the batch run failed? Write it to the log file so it can be browsed at 
leisure and is permanent and unlikely to be scrolled off the screen or 
missed. (Ironically, I use DISPLAY to write logs in Fujitsu COBOL, but don't 
tell anyone... :-))

I'm OK with you taking my name off the code if you feel more comfortable 
with that, although I can't see anything on the site that suggests you can't 
post a joint effort (and it gives visitors TWO people to beat up about the 
code... :-)

If you intend to retain the progressive DISPLAYs, PLEASE take my name off 
it... :-)

No matter WHAT you post, be prepared for the fact that someone won't like it 
:-)

Regards,

Pete.

If you post it as a joint effort, I think you should retain the author line 
as shown above.


>
> Kind regards,
> Bruce.
```

## Thread: OT: Earthquakes — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: Earthquakes

- **From:** Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@googlemail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-10-19T12:05:44-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<af9a5a13-b83a-4c07-b7b5-b304c08f96cc@26g2000yqv.googlegroups.com>`

```
Pete - how was the aftershock?
```

#### ↳ Re: Earthquakes

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-20T15:57:19+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8i744hFn0qU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<af9a5a13-b83a-4c07-b7b5-b304c08f96cc@26g2000yqv.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> Pete - how was the aftershock?

Thanks for asking.

It is simply a tragedy.

For the last month the people of Christchurch have been subject to 
aftershocks on a regular basis. It is quite terrifying and I have friends 
there who are not easily rattled, but the last time I Skyped them I could 
see that they are visibly disturbed by it.

Just when people think it is settling down and a degree of normalcy is 
restored, another series of aftershocks hits them.

The one you are referring to 2 days ago was over 5 on the Richter scale and 
it was situated at a depth of 9 kilometres directly under the city. Being 
that close to the surface (usually they are more than 30 KM deep) makes the 
effect like a magnitude 7.  More buildings were damaged, but worst of all, 
recently restored services and buildings were hit again and this has a 
devastating effect on people's morale.

So far, the estimate of cost for these earthquakes is over $3 billion and 
rising. (We have a total population of just over 4 million).

The people of Christchurch (about 350,000 of them...) 
http://www.christchurch.org.nz/About/ have had a huge amount to endure and 
are doing so with courage and dignity. The stories of people helping each 
other, the heroism amidst carnage, and the untiring efforts to restore vital 
services like water, electricity, phones, and waste disposal are really 
heartwarming and quite humbling. Reconstruction has already started and so 
it is even more painful when these efforts are damaged by further tremors.

That there have been no deaths is simply miraculous and a tribute to the 
systems we have in place for dealing with emergencies like this, the 
readiness and willingness of our Civil Defence, the proper construction of 
buildings, and the general attitude of Kiwis in crisis. Fund raising 
benefits and concerts are going on all over the country, donations from 
businesses and individuals are raising millions but it is a drop in the 
bucket compared to what is needed.

The predictions are that there will be continuing tremors for some months, 
but nobody expected the depth and location of the most recent one.

Life in Christchurch goes on. Schools are open, people are back at work and 
cleaning up is a background task.

But the emotional scars will take much longer to heal.

Pete.
```

## Thread: OT: PC Question — 8 this month / 9 total

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: PC Question

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2010-10-20T10:28:49-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<F92dnbzsNOkSYSPRnZ2dnUVZ_u6dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
My PC has the following hardware:

C: Local Disk
D: DVD Drive
E: DVD-RW Drive
F: External hard Drive

So now I have a Mobikey USB device that allows me to work from home.  The 
Mobikey has zero free bytes so you cannot write to it. If I insert the 
Mobikey and boot the computer the Mobikey is assiged as the F: drive and the 
computer does not see the external hard drive at all.  If I boot the 
computer first and insert the Mobikey later it still takes over the F: drive 
letter and I receive messages about the failure of delayed writes to the 
external (F:) hard drive.

Any sugggestion on what I can do to resolve this?  So far I just do without 
the external hard drive during the day and when I am done working I turn off 
the computer, take out the Mobikey and restart the computer.  This works for 
me because the external drive is just being used for backup of files and 
there is plenty of time after work for any backups to be done.  What I do 
not like is having to daily insert and remove the Mobikey which increases 
the possibilty of trying to insert it backwards bending the pins in the USB 
port.  Thanks in advance for any suggestions.
```

#### ↳ Re: PC Question

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-21T04:35:19+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8i8ghoFodhU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<F92dnbzsNOkSYSPRnZ2dnUVZ_u6dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> My PC has the following hardware:
>
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> advance for any suggestions.

Need more information, Charlie.

What OS?

Is the external drive Firewire, USB, Scuzzy, IDE...?

You CAN override Plug n Play and make drive assignments (given an OS that 
supports this...) but it looks as though someone has gone to a lot of 
trouble to ensure your Mobikey is assigned to drive F:

Changing it might stop it working altogether.

Talk to the tech support where you work and tell them your problem.

Without more on the Mobikey internals it is too risky to re-assign it.

Even if you succeeded you might be on a sticky wicket having to explain why 
you messed with a security device...

Let them do it.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: PC Question

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-21T11:51:50+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8i9a47FprpU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<F92dnbzsNOkSYSPRnZ2dnUVZ_u6dnZ2d@earthlink.com> <8i8ghoFodhU1@mid.individual.net> <d46dnZePrP4y_SLRnZ2dnUVZ_qGdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:8i8ghoFodhU1@mid.individual.net...
…[51 more quoted lines elided]…
> The external drive is plugged into a USB port.

If both the Mobikey and the external drive are USB devices they should be 
individually recognised by PnP when you connect them. You should hear a 
sound when they are connected and disconnected and it is OK to connect and 
disconnect them "warm"  (You don't have to power down or reboot).

If you don't hear the audio confirmation (and assuming you have working 
audio on your system) then either the USB hub or PnP are malfunctioning.

Try This:

1. Disconnect both devices. Disconnect your USB cradle.
2. Reboot from power Off (Cold start).
3. Reconnect the External drive, directly to a port on the machine.
    Did you hear an audio acknowledgement? Did you see a pop-up on the task 
bar?
    If no, open up Start>control panel>system>Hardware>attached devices  and 
see if any entries under USB have a little yellow exclamation mark beside 
them. If they do you may need to re-install USB drivers. 
http://www.softwarepatch.com/tips/usb2-driver.html
USB 2 is much smarter than the earlier versions and is worth installing.
If installing USB 2.0 drivers doesn't fix it, and your external disk is 
still unrecognised try this: http://support.microsoft.com/kb/925196

    If you hear the audio confirmation, then attach the Mobikey directly to 
a port on the machine.

4.  Did you hear an audio condirmation that the Mobikey was found by PnP?

If you did, then drives will have been assigned to both devices and your 
problem should be solved. If you didn't, and you have USB 2.0 drivers 
installed (see above) then the problem is probably with PnP. You will have 
to detect the device manually so it can be properly assigned. Mobikey does 
not require anything on your machine other than a working USB port, a 
working Internet connection, and 20 MB of available memory.

I suspect that your problem is with the USB drivers and (re-)installing USB 
2.0 drivers should solve it.

>
> Actually, today I see the Mobikey is assigned the letter G:.  However
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> inserst/remove the Mobikey.

USB cradles CAN be problematic, although once proper drivers are in place, 
they shouldn't be. That was why I advised NOT using the cradle for the above 
scenario, and connecting directly to machine ports. Once everything is 
working properly, you could try connecting the cradle.
>
> Next I will mention something about my Access 2003 project. This OT
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> it.  I guess it will take me some time to figure all of this out.

It is quite usual for LANs to have 10s, 100s of thousands, or even millions 
of files. The laptop I am writing this on has 1.4 million files on it and 
still has 100 GB of disk free... My LAN has around 3 million files on it. 
Fortunately, the days when we needed to print out a directory listing are 
long gone.

The good news is that you will only be interested in a very small subset at 
any given moment, so there will be some directories you frequent much more 
than others. Don't be intimidated by what is there; learn to search 
directories for what you need.

Good Luck!

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Micro Focus

- **From:** James Gavan <jgavan@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2010-10-23T12:30:08-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<UQFwo.14$kS.3@newsfe17.iad>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<dc296845-4cc7-4f6d-8c35-48c13ada3ef6@c10g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<fdc83baa-1c4b-485e-8420-f189e9a73ce3@h7g2000yqn.googlegroups.com> <dc296845-4cc7-4f6d-8c35-48c13ada3ef6@c10g2000yqh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Oct 21, 12:50 pm, Hugh Porter <shug.por...@googlemail.com> wrote:
> 
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> discrete projects.

You have already expanded on your above statement about the UK company 
and Bill has also indicated reasons for any lack of success.

I'm concentrating on the last sentence - discrete projects. I get the 
impression that you are not too excited about IDEs; you're not the only 
one, I can recall at least two others have expressed that opinion over 
the years.

Yes, if you have a project Accounts Payable/Bought Ledger, then you have 
a customized project, but when you go into real-time production mode you 
have various DLLs, some specific to M/F utilities and those which you 
may build from your own source.

I'm almost there on a OO version of 'Rosetta - Bottles of Beer', using 
the guts of what Bruce and Pete posted - it would be difficult to fault 
the logic they used, with Pete enhancing it by using the indices to get 
at the entries in the Name tables. Yes, it will include a recursive 
invoke of a method.

Nevertheless I sympathize with your complaint that "It's a drag to go 
through output of all the permutations". Pete offered 'scripting' as a 
'quickie' solution. But bearing your comments in mind the OO version 
will allow for (a) display to screen, (b) write to a LineSequential file 
and (c) I'll include a dialog containing just a Listbox that you can 
happily whizz through using the horizontal scroll bar. (I'm using N/E V 
3.1 and I don't think you will have any problems compiling it in your 
Personal Edition).

Firstly I'm not working to produce DLLs or EXEs, just the source to be 
run in Animator mode. That being said, how is the project constructed.

1.  Naturally I have BottlesOfBeer.cbl and a Trigger.cbl to kick start 
the project.

2. However, both options (b) and (c) above require additional source 
code. I want to reuse my own generic classes which already exist. Just 
take '(b) write to a LineSequential file'; I need the following 
additional sources (classes) :-

- LineSequentialFile
- MyErrors
- ErrorMessages

To get around this I have two directories/folders :-

c:\Cobol\Examples\BottlesOfBeer
c:\Copylib

\Copylib contains the source of File, Errors and Messages above, 
including any copyfiles those sources also use. I do compile in \Copylib 
but can't run from that project because the sources are invariably 
invoked elsewhere.

The source for the three above appear in \BottlesOfBeer - using
- LineSequentialFile - the source is in \Copylib but it also appears in 
the BottlesOfBeer project as follows, with its own reference in the 
Bottles project IDE :-

*>---------------------- LineSequentialFile.cbl ------------------------
copy "\Copylib\LineSequentialFile.cbl".
*>----------------------------------------------------------------------

No reference to the Disk in the above - it assumes you are using the 
same drive for \Copylib and \BottlesOfBeer. Within the Bottles project 
use ALT+F2 on the copy line and you display the source for 
LineSequentialFile, which can be edited in either \Copylib, (if within 
that project) or in \BottlesOfBeer, as indicated. Should you edit within 
\BottlesOfBeer - you re-compile in that Folder which outputs IDY and INT 
files into \BottlesOfBeer\DEBUGg. At some stage you should also 
re-compile within \Copylib, (has its own DEBUG sub-folder), because the 
editing changes are reflected in the only source which is in \Copylib. 
Within either of the projects, when you bring up an amended source, M/F 
gives you a warning message that the source has been changed - so 
re-compile within that project.

Now given that I may reach some 30 demo projects, it follows that if I 
change a copyfile or source in \Copylib it is highly likely it will 
affect more than one of my demo projects. That means I have to recompile 
or REBULD ALL for some projects - but bear in mind I'm not in 
'Production mode' and in the real-world, it is highly unlikely you would 
be dealing with some 30 separate projects at one time.

May not be the only way of getting around an IDEs limitations for a 
project, but it works fine for me.

At the current time \Copylib is a 'grab-bag' of diverse sources which 
currently are :-

xxx.cbl - some 55
xxx.cpy - currently 19

I anticipate the above numbers will grow, but wouldn't guesstimate at 
this stage.

It follows that when you go live with a RELEASE compile, either EXEs or 
DLLs, then you may want to extract all, or selective sources from 
\Copylib and put them into logical groupings for separate DLLs.

This is not programming, but systems design - gotta think outside of the 
box, Alistair.

Jimmy
```

### Re: Micro Focus

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2010-10-22T15:43:38-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4Imwo.345993$rT.95571@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<fdc83baa-1c4b-485e-8420-f189e9a73ce3@h7g2000yqn.googlegroups.com> <dc296845-4cc7-4f6d-8c35-48c13ada3ef6@c10g2000yqh.googlegroups.com> <6c41a1b1-a79a-43d1-b897-f45c5285be2f@t20g2000yqa.googlegroups.com>`

```
"HansJ" <hans.igel@googlemail.com> wrote in message 
news:6c41a1b1-a79a-43d1-b897-f45c5285be2f@t20g2000yqa.googlegroups.com...
On 21 Okt., 18:25, Alistair Maclean
<alistair.j.l.macl...@googlemail.com> wrote:
> On Oct 21, 12:50 pm, Hugh Porter <shug.por...@googlemail.com> wrote:
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> discrete projects.

>Alistair, I think that I'm missing the point somehow (which is
> obviously my problem). What is too difficult and why would anyone
> "ditch" his MF licenses as they do cost something?
>
>Regards Hans

I have run into this "issue" more with those using MF for development than 
for those migrating off the mainframe and to Unix/Linux/Windows.


Having worked on "both sides" (customer and MF employee) and having worked 
with other "off-loading" of mainframe development, the "usual" problem is 
that mainframe developers are used to their existing (mainframe) testing and 
development environment. They also expect there to be "systems programmers" 
who maintain and set up the environment.


Duplicating a complex mainframe environment on each PC or even on a network 
takes extensive work. (This is not just for Micro focus, but for all 
workstation development platforms). There are things like (and this is NOT 
an exhaustive list)


1) JCL, Utilities, Source control systems, etc

2) IMS, CICS, and DB2 "control" settings and facilities (test regions, etc)

3) Test data (and all related files)


I have seen a number of "development off-loading" projects fail, because it 
was expected that every developer needed to "set up" their entire 
development environment - as opposed to "status quo" development (on the 
mainframe) where everything is already set up and the tools are what the 
programmer was "used to".


When it comes to actual migration of (production) applications from the 
mainframe to the "workstation" or "network", this is USUALLY (but not 
always) less of an issue. Usually appropriate resources (knowledgeable) 
people are either allocated or hired to figure out what needs to be done to 
"move" the production environment from the mainframe to the 
workstation/network. This is viewed as a "one-time" project and WHEN/IF the 
resources are allocated, then the migration often (usually) succeeds. 
However, even here, if mid-/upper-management has been "sold" that this is a 
transparent migration and that the vendor provides all the tools to make 
this "easy", then I have seen this fail as well.


This may or may not be what Alistair was referring to, but it does express 
when I have seen such project fail or flounder.
```

```
On 22 Okt., 22:43, "Bill Klein" <wmkl...@noreply.ix.netcom.com> wrote:
> "HansJ" <hans.i...@googlemail.com> wrote in message
>
…[66 more quoted lines elided]…
> when I have seen such project fail or flounder.

Bill,

it is abolutely correct what you said.

Depending on the requirements for a test environment, there is nothing
better as the same platform with the same software versions.

On the other hand, there are situations where a system coming close is
good enough. I can recall a PC based environment we had for developing
online applications for Unisys mainframes and this worked well enough
to get application development up to a point where it could be copied
to the maninframe, compiled and did work. Proper testing had however
still to be done within the target environment, no doubt about that.

I have never experienced myself a mainframe migration project to fail,
though I know of quite some that did.

So it is all a question of how this is approached and what technology
is used.

If you go to a butcher for a haircut, you should not be surprised
about the outcome, though it does not imply that there are no butchers
doing it good enough.
```

```
> Street (otherwise known as Sweeny Todd)?
```

```
to a professional with the right profession, it does not mean you are
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OO vetrsus structured was Re: Not (intended to be) a "Commercial"

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-25T16:17:48+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8ikb6uF4laU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<GbBko.157427$Is6.93397@en-nntp-13.dc1.easynews.com> <8fhil7FohoU1@mid.individual.net> <kHRko.162710$mN7.155152@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <8fiv2tF8abU1@mid.individual.net> <BuYko.164603$mN7.5178@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <8fjl7bF2e6U1@mid.individual.net> <7kse96p4kccr6f0jg1t3c1bgfj0vccp9r3@4ax.com> <8frlvoFc8oU1@mid.individual.net> <4nh9c656t5913134mkam1jm2ebcqvvs3q6@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Wed, 22 Sep 2010 00:15:49 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[44 more quoted lines elided]…
> Clark Morris

Back in the mid 1990s, when it started to become apparent to me that the 
rest of the world was not going in the same direction as Fortress COBOL, I 
figured they might have a point worth investigating.

It took a number of years for the real understanding to gel.

There were a handful of us who thought that OO COBOL might be some use but 
the general response (at least from this forum) was lofty, scornful, and, at 
times, simply abusive.

I felt like a voice crying in the Wilderness and eventually shut up. :-) 
(The only GOOD thing about banging your head against the wall is that it is 
nice when you stop... :-))

To be fair, I probably provoked some of the bad reaction (although I never 
posted here with a mischievous intent to "stir") and although it was an 
unpleasant experience, I came out of it more mature and tolerant. I came to 
realise that it really doesn't matter; it is only computer programming, not 
life and death.

Having worked for decades on IBM (mainly, but also "other") mainframes, I 
well understood the things you mention in your post. There certainly WAS 
Management resistance, but it was largely a reflection of Programmer 
resistance, and all the reasons for NOT doing it that you mention above, in 
retrospect, were quite valid. (However, in some places, there was an 
unfortunate arrogance as well which saw PCs as "toys" and the Internet as a 
stupid fad that would soon go away...)

Nevertheless, I persevered with it for my own development and found it (at 
least when combined with a component based approach) to be very effective 
and useful. I wrote OO COBOL components for over 10 years and am still using 
some of them.

What few of us actually realised at the time was that the Network would take 
over the world, permeate in subtle ways so many different aspects of our 
lives, and usher in a completely new kind of society with different models 
for trading.

And THAT is the nub of it.

The network is about Objects and Layers.

It likes small, encapsulated pieces of functionality that can be easily 
invoked locally or remotely. and run on the nearest available processor. It 
is about sharing and redirecting workloads and resources dynamically.

The idea of a monolithic sequential process which directly controls 
everything it does, is just anathema to it.

Batch Processing is only a requirement in a certain mindset. It IS NOT 
essential if systems are designed differently.

(If your system reflects an analogue of the real world at every given 
moment, with all transactions complete or pending action at a certain 
specific time in the future, there is no need to "complete" transactional 
updates overnight because you didn't have the capacity to complete them at 
the time they happened.

This was one of the reasons that Batch Processing came into being; it simply 
wasn't possible to complete the processing for the volume of transactions 
occurring, as they occurred. The on-line system simply wrote down keys and 
they were picked up and processed overnight. It was like "finishing off " 
the transactions at a less busy time...

Another oft-quoted-reason is that we need to view things as of certain dates 
and the "only way" to do that is to accumulate and consolidate the 
transactions in the stated period, which requires overnight dedicated 
processing.

It doesn't.

Modern Data warehousing and newer retrieval techniques can consolidate the 
details as part of a retrieval request. Stored procedures on a database can 
offline this and work transparently.

We have better ways of doing things and much more powerful processors and 
subsytems to do them with. Batch Processing is simply archaic.)

So, if you eliminate Batch Processing, why would you need a centralised 
procedural processing system?

You don't, and most of the world is aware of that. More are gaining that 
awareness every day.

COBOL is ideal for the centralised Batch Processing model; OO is ideal for 
encapsulated functionality that can run anywhere.

You can figure out for yourself which of these models is most likely to have 
a future.

By-the-way, I have no regrets about moving to OO, not even about moving to 
OO COBOL. It was very useful and helped me transition to much more powerful 
stuff.

And I have no grudges over the flames that went down 15 years ago... :-)

Pete.
```

<a id="t-797344853b"></a>
## Thread: Dealing with Projects — 5 this month / 13 total

### Dealing with Projects

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-27T00:55:49+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8intu7FmsqU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
A few months back I posted here asking if anyone had some simple code to
batch conpile Fujitsu Projects.

The Fujitsu docs have a sample (EXTREMELY primitive) DOS batch file which
can do it for PowerCOBOL and HeyBub posted a better example, based on the
Fujitsu one. (They don't provide anything I could find for NetCOBOL)

Recently, Jimmy Gavan raised the point about building releases from projects
for Micro Focus, and observed, very correctly, that organizing this  is part
of the wider system design, or architecture.

In my "spare time" over the past few months  I have been looking at this for
some Fujitsu clients of PRIMA, but I think the same applies to any vendor
where the IDE uses "Projects".

Here's a brief summary of the situation:

1. People developing software for delivery to PC platforms often choose to
build related functions into .DLLs.

2. A "project" will deal with source (COBOL in the cases we are talking
about, but it doesn't have to be...), and it will produce executables in the
form of .DLL or .EXE files. For a simple application (like "Bottles of
Beer") a single project is enough, but consider an application that deals
with say, hospitality. The system has to deal with bookings and
reservations, accounting, restaurant(s), front office functions, back office
functions, tours, groups, ski hire,  phone calls, movie hire, and so on.
Obviously, it would be foolish to build all of this under one project.
Instead, the core is placed into a single project in a "base" directory, and
the other functions (sub-systems) go into subdirectories as separate
projects.

Typically, a system like this would have around 70 projects which produce
around 300 .DLLs and .EXEs.

Some of the projects will involve GUI applications, and for Fujitsu
customers this often means PowerCOBOL. Other projects deal with batch
processing or providing common functions and these are built in Net COBOL.
So, in order to deliver a new release of the entire system, a number of
PowerCOBOL and NetCOBOL projects need to be built. The IDE enables projects
to be built with a single mouse click, but it is pretty tedious if you have
70 projects, to have to open up each one and click on the "build" icon (then
check the results).

3. Then there are the COPY and INCLUDE Libraries. Each project can indicate
where the COPYbooks it uses are located. It can be done by a separate
configuration file that is included in the project, or there are options
within the IDE to specify paths as compiler options, or you can use an
Environment Variable, and any of these is much better than hard-coding COPY
library paths in the source code. Often people will place the COPY libraries
onto a single server and the workstations map to it. The point is, that
unless the projects can find the libraries, there will be no executables
produced.

4. A couple of my clients were interested in something to deal with this
release build problem. One of them found that to build a new release of
their package needed hours to run and if it was left overnight, it could
fail, and even if it didn't, it was a nightmare to go through all the
listings and see what failed and what succeeded, bearing in mind these
listings could be distributed across a directory hierarchy.

As a result, I started working on a "Projects Manager" tool.

I finished it yesterday :-)

(At the moment, it deals only with Fujitsu Projects (PowerCOBOL and
NetCOBOL), although I'd consider doing a Micro Focus version if people were
interested.)

During the last few weeks I have had some people who have been kind enough
to test what I had, and their feedback has been incorporated into the
finished product. As this is a programming forum, I'll comment on the
programming...

My early efforts were pretty stumbling and there was no multithreading, so
while the batch processes ran, the GUI was frozen and looked like it had
hung :-). Mouse clicks were ignored and so on...

I was surprised to find that win XP does not (of itself)
"remember" the last size and position of an application window, so this had
to be coded into the tool, and the whole development was an adjunct to my
self-education in  more advanced C#. The tool accesses the registry to
detect the presence and version of COBOL in use, it kicks off and monitors
each compile as a task WITHOUT using DOS shell, and knows immediately if a
compile succeeded or failed (no waiting until everything is finished).
Progressive information is posted to the Screen in real time. It also
gathers, collates, and wraps the reporting from disparate directories  where
projects are located and provides a single consolidated summary, or separate
reports for each project, all provided in the base directory.

It has confirmed my previous opinion that .Net simply rocks. (C# is really
just a vehicle for the underlying power of the Framework) and, as my
confidence in it grows, C# is the most enjoyable language to write that I
have ever used. You can do really cool things really quickly :-)

I loved using COBOL, and it has a simplicity and elegance that makes it
attractive and easy to write, but it just doesn't have the succinctness or
(more importantly) the sheer power that I get with C#. Tasks that would take
a lot of time in COBOL (first to find out how to do it and what API calls
were necessary, then to get the interface to the API working correctly) are
provided by Intellisense in Visual Studio, with method signatures
documented, exactly when and where you need them.

When I found out the problem with window resizing not being remembered, I
had no idea where to start. Straight to GOOGLE and in 10 minutes I had cut,
pasted, and modified to my exact requirements, sample C# code. I couldn't do
that in COBOL. The sample base just isn't there, if you want to do anything
a little less ordinary. (This is why I used to get really annoyed with
programmers who said: "Anything I want to do, I can do in COBOL".... it
shows such limited imagination :-).)

The final release of the tool incorporates multithreading  (accomplished by
asynchronous delegates in C# (although there are other ways to do it as
well) - very interesting and kept me fully occupied for several days,
researchng and testing until I got the hang of it :-). Unlike a cut and
paste of some sample code, I wanted the full background and conceptual
understanding on this, so it meant hitting the books...), and multi tasking.

The tool can run multiple instances of itself so large package builds can be
multi-processed with several subsytems being built concurrently. It can
recurse directories to find the subsystem projects, and it can operate
across a LAN with projects and libraries on different drives.

I received the following email from a lead programmer:

"Hi Pete,

Congratulations - The tool is fantastic!
As <name suppressed> said we ran the batch compile on the slowest PC & it
actually took only about one hour to process the 75 projects selected (have
both Powercobol & Netcobol). It successfully compiled about 296 dll/exe, it
would take me at least half a day to do the same job manually. "

I was really pleased by this, as this girl is one of the most capable IT
people I have ever seen (and one of the best COBOL Programmers), and half a
day of her time would be a day and a half of most people's... :-)

I have other emails (all unsolicited), all very impressed and saying it
would make life much easier (which was the main reason I wrote it), but
enough self indulgence... :-)

The tool has too many features to enumerate here but if you'd like to have a
look at it or find out more, here's a link:

http://primacomputing.co.nz/PRIMAWebSite/BatchCompile.aspx

(If you run the embedded video on the page linked to above, you will get to 
spend 15 minutes with me, in my world. (Remember, surreal experiences are 
good for personal growth... :-))

Getting back to the point...

Deciding how to organize your projects and deciding what will BE in a
project, is a major consideration, and all-to-often, overlooked.

Too many projects and you have a very tedious task to build a release; too
few, and you have too many eggs in each basket...

It isn't the fault of the IDE. It doesn't matter whether you use Fujitsu's
IDEs for PowerCOBOL and NetCOBOL, Eclipse, Micro Focus, Visual Studio, or
anything else, the fact is that careful thought should be given to the
overall packaging and architecture of your application right from day one.

If you let projects just "grow like Topsy" it isn't long before you can't
find stuff , or there are COPY conflicts or  you miss the fact that one
build out of the many didn't succeed, you deploy your package and have a
very disgruntled customer with a problem that is not easy to run down.

Using "Projects" is a step up from building everything at a command line,
but the projects themselves are also components in your overall architecture
and deserve the same consderation you would give components at the 
programming
level.

Pete.
```

```
Again this is a case of the sample base being non-existent and the
culture of COBOL not being as sharing as either the Assembler culture
(check www.cbttape.org for IBM z series and before dating back to the
Michmods tapes for OS360 back in the 1970's) or the C/C++/C# cultures.

>

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>understanding on this, so it meant hitting the books...), and multi tasking.

If I were doing development using IDEs and projects instead of being
retired, I would be very interested in your tool and my comments have
nothing to do with the value of it.  Also given the realities of the
environment and the intended platforms C# would probably be my choice.
The question that I raise is how much of the power has to do with the
IDE and development environment as opposed to either the language or
even the OO paradigm.

Clark Morris
>

>Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Is it the language or the IDE was Re: Dealing with Projects

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-10-27T12:53:26+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8ip7voFn3uU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<8intu7FmsqU1@mid.individual.net> <hhudc617h8r5fnlo8j2jv96gbuo96b5v46@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Wed, 27 Oct 2010 00:55:49 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> COBOL with the same grace, would this be true?

That's a very good question, Clark and I thought about this at length when I 
first got my head around VS and C#.

The new product from Micro Focus DOES have these advantages for COBOL (as 
does Fujitsu NetCOBOL for .Net) and you can run both of these COBOLs in 
either Visual Studio or Eclipse.

At first, I kind of wished that the same environment I was enjoying in C# 
was available in COBOL. (Now, it is, at least for people who fork out for a 
CLR generating compiler.) But, as time goes by, here's what happens...(all 
comments based on personal experience; YMMV...)

1.You start to realise that even if you DID have the same facilities in 
COBOL there is still a lot of writing required...
2. There are facilities in the other languages that simply AREN'T in COBOL. 
Even with a version of COBOL which opens up the whole .Net framework, there 
are still things that COBL simply cannot do. It wasn't designed for an OO 
environment. You can push it into one, using the amazing OO COBOL features 
provided by both Micro Focus and Fujitsu, but it is still Jake the Peg (with 
his extra leg... http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KJleJbn9G6Y ).
3. As time goes by and you find yourself becoming more facile with the new 
language you realise there is SO much that COBOL doesn't even look at.  Some 
Examples?

1. For me, I was getting tired of embedded SQL which is tiresome and 
primitive in today's world. C# opens up LINQ (Language integrated queries) 
which is a whole new ball game for data access. (Note: this is not a .Net 
Framework facility; it is a LANGUAGE facility and represents a completely 
new way  (largely driven by Anders Haljsberg) of looking at how we query 
information.) Currently, it supports (and generates) SQL under the covers, 
but it uses the concepts of functional programming to only hit the disk at 
the last minute (JIT) and you can manipulate queries without ever actually 
doing them... :-) With the arrival of new data storage devices and 
subsystems, LINQ is poised  to lever the advantages of new facilities in 
hardware without having to keep everything tied back to SQL for 
compatibility.

2. Taking the current example of the Projects management tool. If I had 
written it in PowerCOBOL, I would have been stuck with a frozen GUI as the 
background batch processes executed. (Or I would have been off into another 
excursion into the Windows API for thread management) C# offers delegation 
seamlessly, and in a single line of code I can create delegate methods that 
can be used for all kinds of purposes (not just multithreading). I like to 
write code that is generalised so I have a fighting chance of being able to 
reuse it. With a delegate method I can set up a generic signature or 
interface and  run multiple objects through the same "controller". I can't 
do that in COBOL.

3. I needed access to the registry (to check what levels of COBOL are 
installed locally. 3 lines of C#. In COBOL, an API call and all the fiddling 
with getting the right definitions in the interface . (Certainly, CLR 
generating versions of COBOL can use the .Net Framework to do this just as 
easily as C#, but I don't have, want, or need an $11,000 compiler when C# is 
free.)

Over the last couple of years I have been moved to the inexorable conclusion 
that there are free tools out there which are streets ahead of COBOL for the 
Networked environment I am developing for and using myself. The support is 
better (and free), and I don't have an arrogant vendor who treats me as a 
"locked in" prisoner who will take what he's given and be thankful for it.

As a small Company, PRIMA has to consider what it spends on tools. Even when 
I WAS prepared (and had the funds allocated) to buy into NetCOBOL for .Net, 
it was not a decision that caused me joy. Thankfully, events transpired to 
change my mind and the money I saved was far better spent elsewhere. (I also 
came to the realisation that such compilers are actually pointless, but that 
is another story...)

I can't afford to invest in stuff unless I know it will pay for itself. (I 
replace my notebook every 3 years or so, but I check that the one I am using 
has paid for itself, before doing so... :-) )  A CLR generating compiler 
could never compete with a free language like C# or VB.Net, on price, never 
mind functionality.

All that is required for membership in this club is the desire to join it, 
and the determination to allocate some time for re-education. (Everything I 
know about the environment I am using came from books, videos, analysing 
sample code, asking questions in forums, and simply trying stuff out.  I 
have had no formal training in C#, but I manage to produce applications 
OK...(To be fair, I DID have a basic background in OOP, gained using OO 
COBOL to write Component Classes over a number of years.) Fortunately, it is 
an ongoing process; you don't have to "learn C#" overnight.  I started with 
some basic free videos on VS and progressed from there (at my own pace). 
Within a few days I was writing simple applications and actually chuckling 
at how much fun it was :-)

Never mind. I have reached a time now where I see no need to evangelize and 
whatever people use is fine by me. :-)

I have documented my experiences here and elsewhere just so people can know 
they have a choice; I really odn't care whether they exercise it or not. (I 
am glad to help anyone who does, though :-))


>>
>> When I found out the problem with window resizing not being
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> even the OO paradigm.

I recently reviewed (on video) the excellent new tool released by MicroFocus 
(after Bill gave us a "heads up" here).It irritated  me a little bit that 
most of the "features" they were claiming, were nothing to do with COBOL, 
they were "features" that are enjoyed by all of the .Net languages as part 
of the framework and Visual Studion 2010. While this may be exciting and 
amazing to COBOL guys who have never seen it, for the rest of us it is a bit 
of a yawn.

I had a follow up from Micro Focus asking me about it and I responded 
bluntly, but fairly. (I actually think they have done a really excelllent 
job of integrating COBOL into this environment and the tool is in line with 
the other high quality products they have produced in the past.)

We ended up having a conversation and it was interesting and pleasant. I 
won't break confidence by revealing private conversations here, but it was a 
pleasure to correspond with them and the respect I have always had for the 
company (apart from the VISOC fiasco, which was a long time ago, and has to 
be let go, sometime... :-)) was reinforced .

I can see many of their customers being very glad to see this product.

However, I don't think it is for SMEs (like me), and, as I am on record as 
stating here previously, I believe that any solution which perpetuates COBOL 
has no future.

This is the real nettle which needs to be grasped and promoting CLR 
compilers for COBOL doesn't grasp it.

Pete.
```

### OT: Anyone know how to buy a used iSeries machine and get a good deal?

- **From:** Paul <paul-nospamatall.raulerson@mac.com>
- **Date:** 2010-10-27T22:25:09-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4cc8ed15$0$14832$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
I know this is a bit off topic, but not entirely!  The program I need 
to work on is mostly COBOL. :)

I need to buy a nice little used machine, and it needs to have the 
compilers on it. Prices for machines range from $500 to $6000 - 
depending upon who you talk to.

And every single vendor tells me they are cutting me a great deal, but 
as soon as I mention I need the compilers, zap.... they tend to run 
away.

Or they point me to ancient machines, running OS 4.3 for $1200.  Which 
is what I might just have to deal with!

Anyone know of any reliable vendors, at least someone who won't give me 
the used car salesman runaround?

Budget for this is skinning a very tight $2000. We started at $1000, 
but quickly found out that nobody would talk to us at that level!

As usual, this has a bit to do with our favorite charity, though at 
$2K, I very much plan on finding a way to recoup a little bit of that 
cost with consulting or something.

Thanks
-Paul

P.S.  I wasn't thinking about it, but if anyone here has one they want 
to part with...
```

```
Have you thought about renting or leasing a refurbished machine? Might

Sometimes getting parts from machines intended for scrap can be very viable, 
(but it takes time and phone calls to local vendors. I found that if they 
know it is for charity they will sometimes be very generous...). I built 
several PCs for disabled people from parts nobody wanted (got a 200GB hard 
drive for $10...) ending up with some great value. I built a really good one 
for a lady with MS, so she could do a writing course by correspondence. She 
had it for years and passed it to her grandson who is still using it. (It 
cost a total of $150, loaded with Office 2003, Win XP, and 56KB modem. The 
software was OEM or second-hand.)

If your charity is likely to be ongoing for the duration, leasingcould be an 
attractive option. A leasing company can get a tax break on leasing to a 
charity and some of them may be sympathetic. Try and get them cover in a 
local newspaper for example...

>
> Anyone know of any reliable vendors, at least someone who won't give
> me the used car salesman runaround?

I know of one vendor here, but I don't think it would be any cheaper than 
you have been quoted and then there is shipping...

>
> Budget for this is skinning a very tight $2000. We started at $1000,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> cost with consulting or something.

Working for Charity (I do a small amount) is rewarding in itself but you 
have to accept that it will cost you time and money. Sometimes, other people 
who are in, or support, the charity have contacts who can help. Get the word 
around. You may be surprised...

Good luck!

Pete.
```
